\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 160 159
\end{verbatim}

Welche der folgenden Aussage(n) ist/sind korrekt (0-4 korrekte Antworten)?

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Der Q-Q-Plot der kombinierten Körpergrößen lässt auf eine Normalverteilung schließen.
\item
  Der Q-Q-Plot der Frauen-Körpergrößen ist kompatibel mit einer Normalverteilung.
\item
  Der Q-Q-Plot der Kinder-Körpergrößen ist kompatibel mit einer Normalverteilung.
\item
  Das Histogramm der Kinder-Körpergrößen zeigt eine bimodale Verteilung.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Frage 3 - Korrelation}\label{frage-3---korrelation}

Gegeben sei folgende Stichprobe von Punktepaaren \((x_i, y_i)\) der Variablen \(X\) und \(Y\):

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{_main_files/_main_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-35-1.pdf}}

Welche der folgenden Aussage(n) ist/sind korrekt (0-4 korrekte Antworten)?

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Pearson's Korrelationskoeffizient ist geignet um den Zusammenhang zwischen \(X\) und \(Y\) zu messen.
\item
  Pearson's Korrelationskoeffizient ist gleich 1.
\item
  Spearman's Rangkorrelationskoeffizient ist \(<1\).
\item
  Spearman's Rangkorrelationskoeffizient misst die Stärke des linearen Zusammenhangs zwischen \(X\) und \(Y\).
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Frage 4}\label{frage-4}

Gegeben sei folgende Stichprobe: \(x = (9.83, 13.87, 5.90, 10.56,  6.95, 9.46)\).

Welche der folgenden Aussage(n) ist/sind korrekt (0-4 korrekte Antworten)?

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Median und Mittelwert sind identisch.
\item
  Ein Modus ist \(9.83\).
\item
  Die Stichprobenstandardabweichung ist \(2.420024\).
\item
  Der Korrelationskoeffizient beträgt \(0.78\).
\end{itemize}

\chapter{Bayes statistics}\label{bayes_statistics}

To gain a richer understanding, I can recommend (optionally) reading the introductory chapters in
John Kruschke's book \href{https://nyu-cdsc.github.io/learningr/assets/kruschke_bayesian_in_R.pdf}{Doing Bayesian Data Analysis}.

As stated in the first chapter, Bayesian statistics is based on the idea that
\href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bayesian_probability}{probability} is a measure of our uncertainty about an event or a parameter.
Here, we use \emph{prior} (i.e., before/outside of our experiment) knowledge
about a parameter and \textbf{update} this knowledge with new data using the
famous

\textbf{\href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bayes\%27_theorem}{Bayes' theorem}}:

\[
p(\theta | \text{data}) = \frac{p(\text{data} | \theta) \cdot p(\theta)}{p(\text{data})},
\]

where:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(p(\theta | \text{data})\) is the \textbf{posterior probability (respectively the posterior distribution of the parameter)}:
  the updated probability of the parameter \(\theta\) given the observed data.
\item
  \(p(\text{data} | \theta)\) is the \textbf{likelihood}: the probability of observing the data given a certain value of the parameter \(\theta\).
\item
  \(p(\theta)\) is the \textbf{prior probability (respectively the prior distribution of the parameter)}: the initial belief about the parameter \(\theta\) before seeing the data.
\item
  \(p(\text{data})\) is the \textbf{marginal likelihood} or \textbf{evidence}: the probability of observing the data under all possible parameter values.
\end{itemize}

In our context, \(\theta\) is an effect size, a group difference (\(\mu_1 - \mu_2\)), a correlation (\(\rho\)),
a regression coefficient (\(\beta\)), or a proportion (p)\ldots{}

\section{Derivation of Bayes' theorem}\label{derivation-of-bayes-theorem}

We have already come across Bayes' theorem in the context of conditional probability where we have calculated
the probability of a person having a disease \emph{given} having tested positive for it:

\[
\mathbb{P}(Dpos|Tpos) = \frac{\mathbb{P}(Tpos|Dpos) \cdot \mathbb{P}(Dpos)}{\mathbb{P}(Tpos)}.
\]

The numerator is by definition of the conditional probability just: \(\mathbb{P}(Tpos \cap Dpos)\). Let's put this in:

\[
\mathbb{P}(Dpos|Tpos) = \frac{\mathbb{P}(Tpos \cap Dpos)}{\mathbb{P}(Tpos)}.
\]

which is also true by the definition of conditional probability.

One can rewrite the denominator as:

\[
\mathbb{P}(Tpos) = \mathbb{P}(Tpos|Dpos)\mathbb{P}(Dpos) + \mathbb{P}(Tpos|Dneg)\mathbb{P}(Dneg) = TP + FP,
\]
since one can have a positive test result if one has the disease (true positive) or if one does not have the disease (false positive).
This is the so-called \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Law_of_total_probability}{law of total probability}.
To see this, just draw a binary tree starting with disease status (pos/neg) and test result (pos/neg) as branches, as we did before.

Summarized, we have:

\[
\mathbb{P}(Dpos|Tpos) = \frac{\mathbb{P}(Tpos|Dpos) \cdot 
\mathbb{P}(Dpos)}{\mathbb{P}(Tpos|Dpos) \cdot \mathbb{P}(Dpos) + \mathbb{P}(Tpos|Dneg) \cdot \mathbb{P}(Dneg)}.
\]

We have proven Bayes theorem for the case of a binary test.

Note the simple fact that if \(\mathbb{P}(Dpos)\) (which is the prevalence of the disease)
is small, the probability of having the disease given a positive test result is also small.
In fact, it is arbitrarily small the smaller the prevalence is.
For \(\mathbb{P}(Dpos)=0\), we have \(\mathbb{P}(Dpos|Tpos) = \frac{0}{0 + \mathbb{P}(Tpos|Dneg) \cdot 1} = 0\),
assuming that there can still be false positive test results: \(\mathbb{P}(Tpos|Dneg) > 0\).

We could easily image that there are not only two states reported by a test. Maybe, it is a more sophisticated test reporting 3 or 4 states.

We would just extend the denominator accordingly
(e.g., for a 3-state test with labels \(T_1, T_2, T_3\), where \(T_3\) indicates a high concentration of some component):

\[
\mathbb{P}(Dpos|T_3) = \frac{\mathbb{P}(T_3|Dpos) \cdot 
\mathbb{P}(Dpos)}{\mathbb{P}(T_3|Dpos) \cdot \mathbb{P}(Dpos) + \mathbb{P}(T_3|Dneg) \cdot \mathbb{P}(Dneg)},
\]
which would be the probability that the person has the disease given that the test result is \(T_3\).

In this context, we are interested in the probability of a state of disease.
We estimate this probability based on the test result (\(Tpos\) or \(Tneg\)) and \emph{prior} knowledge about the disease (\(\mathbb{P}(Dpos)\)).
The prior knowledge does not necessarily have to be known \emph{before} the test, \textbf{the point is to combine knowledge in a coherent way}. We will
later see that the order of this combination is not important.

\section{Bayes' theorem in the context of parameter estimation}\label{bayes-theorem-in-the-context-of-parameter-estimation}

If we use the coin flip example, you can always think of probability of a therapy working for instance.
It is a placeholder for a parameter of interest.

A probability distribution outlines all possible outcomes of a random process along with their associated probabilities.
For instance, in the case of a coin toss, the distribution is straightforward: there are two outcomes,
heads and tails, with corresponding probabilities \(\theta\) and \(1-\theta\).
For more complex scenarios, such as measuring the height of a randomly chosen individual,
the distribution is less straightforward. Here, each possible height, say 172 cm or 181 cm,
is assigned a probability as we have discussed in the context of the normal distribution and continuous distributions
in general.

\section{Examples}\label{examples}

We have jumped into the deep end right away with exercise 2 in the previous chapter. Let's now look at some more examples to get a feeling for
how the Bayes theorem works.

\subsection{Example 1 - defective products}\label{example_defective_products}

(Example from Script ``Statistik und Wahrscheinlichkeitstheorie using R'',
S.331 ff, Werner Gurker)

A manufacturer claims that the defect rate of their products is only 5\%, while the customer believes it to be 10\%.
\textbf{Before} the result of a sample inspection is known, we assign both rates an equal 50-50 chance:

\[
\pi(0.05) = \pi(0.10) = 0.5
\]

Assume that in a sample of size 20, there are 3 defective items.
Using the binomial distribution \(B(20, \theta)\), the data information is given as follows:

\[
p(3|\theta = 0.05) = \binom{20}{3} (0.05)^3 (0.95)^{17} = 0.0596
\]

\[
p(3|\theta = 0.10) = \binom{20}{3} (0.10)^3 (0.90)^{17} = 0.1901
\]

The marginal distribution of \(X\) (the number of defective items in the sample) for \(x = 3\) is as follows:

\[
m(3) = p(3|0.05)\pi(0.05) + p(3|0.10)\pi(0.10) = 0.1249
\]

Here, we consider all parameters in the parameter space of interest. We are only interested in \(\theta = 0.05\)
vs.~\(\theta = 0.10\) in this case.

\[
\pi(0.05 \mid X = 3) = \frac{p(3 \mid 0.05) \pi(0.05)}{m(3)} = 0.2387
\]

\[
\pi(0.10 \mid X = 3) = \frac{p(3 \mid 0.10) \pi(0.10)}{m(3)} = \mathbf{0.7613}
\]

A priori, we had no preference for either of the two defect rates. After observing a relatively high
defect rate of \(3/20 = 15\%\) in the sample, the posterior probability for \(\theta = 0.10\) is
approximately three times as likely as \(\theta = 0.05\).

Note that in this example the a priori probabilities were equal:

R codes are in the \href{https://github.com/jdegenfellner/Script_QM1_ZHAW/blob/main/04-Bayes_statistics.Rmd}{git} repository

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{_main_files/_main_files/figure-latex/bar_plot_code-1.pdf}}

\textbf{After} collecting data, we have updated our prior beliefs about the defect rate:

R codes are in the \href{https://github.com/jdegenfellner/Script_QM1_ZHAW/blob/main/04-Bayes_statistics.Rmd}{git} repository

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{_main_files/_main_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-36-1.pdf}}

Let's assume, we know the manufacturer to be rather untrustworthy. Many inspections in the past
revealed higher defect rates than claimed. For simplicity, we still want to decide between the two
defect rates. \(0.05\) and \(0.10\).

How does the calculation change if we assign a much higher probability to the defect rate of \(0.10\)?
See \hyperref[exercise_defective_product_rate]{Exercise 1} for this.

\subsection{Example 2 - extending the defective products example}\label{example_defective_products_extended}

What if we do not want to limit ourselves to the two defect rates of 5\% and 10\%?
We now get creative and assign the following prior probability \textbf{distribution} to the defective rate \(\theta\):

R codes are in the \href{https://github.com/jdegenfellner/Script_QM1_ZHAW/blob/main/04-Bayes_statistics.Rmd}{git} repository

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{_main_files/_main_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-37-1.pdf}}

If we had to guess, we would say that the defect rate is most likely 20\%.
And we are rather sure about this guess. The mode (highest prior probability) of the
\href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beta_distribution}{beta distribution} is at 0.2. We are also pretty sure that the defect rate is not 0\% or above, say, 50\%.
This distribution is an expression of our \textbf{prior beliefs} about the defect rate.

Now, we \textbf{observe 11 defective items in a sample of 20}.
We expect as posterior distribution not a bar plot with two probabilities for the parameter values
\(\theta = 0.05\) and \(\theta = 0.10\), but a \textbf{distribution} of probabilities for all possible defect rates (in the range of 0-1).
Btw. we could play the same game using a coin or the effectiveness of a therapy as example.

The only thing we need to do is to plug in the information into Bayes' theorem to get the posterior distribution.
The calculations would be complex by hand, so we just use R. We want to understand the concept, so calculating by hand
can be put off until later (if at all necessary for us.)

\[
\textit{p}(\theta \mid X = 11) = \frac{\textit{p}(X = 11 \mid \theta) \cdot \text{Beta}(\theta \mid \alpha, \beta)}{m(11)}
\]

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(\text{Beta}(\theta \mid \alpha, \beta)\) is the prior distribution (density) of the defect rate which captures our beliefs about the defect rate.
\item
  \(\textit{p}(X = 11 \mid \theta)\) is the likelihood of observing 11 defective items
  in a sample of 20 given a certain defect rate \(\theta\).
  This would be the density function of a binomial distribution.
\item
  \(m(11)\) is the marginal distribution of the data for \(X = 11\)
  considering \textbf{all} possible defect rates between 0 and 1.
\item
  \(\textit{p}(\theta \mid X = 11)\) is the posterior density of the defect
  rate \emph{given} the observed data.
\end{itemize}

Let's look at the resulting posterior distribution:

R codes are in the \href{https://github.com/jdegenfellner/Script_QM1_ZHAW/blob/main/04-Bayes_statistics.Rmd}{git} repository

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{_main_files/_main_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-38-1.pdf}}

As we can see, the posterior distribution has a new peak at \(0.38\). If we had to guess
now, we would probably say that the defect rate is around 38\%.
We have updated our beliefs about the defect rate based on the new data.
In the graph, there is also the observed defect rate of 55\%. This observed rate (new data)
draws our prior beliefs towards the observed rate.

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{The more data we observe}, the more the posterior distribution would be
  drawn towards the observed rate, because we would be surer due to the large sample size.
\item
  \textbf{The stronger our prior beliefs} in a certain value (or range) of \(\theta\), the less we are
  convinced by new data.
\end{itemize}

One really nice aspect of the Bayesian view is that we get a \textbf{full probability distribution} for
the parameter of interest, given the prior beliefs and the observed data.
We then can elegantly make all kinds of statements when looking at the posterior, like the following:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The probability that \(\theta\) is between \(0.30\) and \(0.50\) is (area under the posterior)
  approximately \(0.80\):

  R codes are in the \href{https://github.com/jdegenfellner/Script_QM1_ZHAW/blob/main/04-Bayes_statistics.Rmd}{git} repository

  \pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{_main_files/_main_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-39-1.pdf}}
\item
  The probability that \(\theta\) is below \(0.25\) is (area under the posterior):
\end{itemize}

R codes are in the \href{https://github.com/jdegenfellner/Script_QM1_ZHAW/blob/main/04-Bayes_statistics.Rmd}{git} repository

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{_main_files/_main_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-40-1.pdf}}

The previous two examples showcase how to \textbf{estimate a proportion} using prior knowledge
and new data.
The fact, that we get a full distribution of the parameter of interest,
is a key feature of Bayesian statistics and (as far as I know) not available
in Frequentist statistics and Null Hypothesis Significance Testing (NHST), which we
will discuss in the next chapter.

\section{Highest Density Intervals (HDI)}\label{highest-density-intervals-hdi}

Apart from taking the mode or mean of the posterior distribution,
another way to \textbf{summarize a posterior distribution} is by using
the \textbf{highest density interval (HDI)}.
The HDI identifies the most credible range of values in a distribution while
covering a specified portion of the distribution, such as 93\%.
The key feature of the HDI is that every point
inside the interval has a higher probability density than any point outside
the interval, making it an effective summary of the most plausible values.

Here is an example for a 93\% HDI for the posterior distribution of \(\theta\):

R codes are in the \href{https://github.com/jdegenfellner/Script_QM1_ZHAW/blob/main/04-Bayes_statistics.Rmd}{git} repository

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{_main_files/_main_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-41-1.pdf}}

\textbf{Interpretation}: The 93\% HDI for the posterior distribution of \(\theta\) is \([0.25, 0.51]\).
With a probability of 93\%, the defect rate is between 25\% and 55\%, given our prior
beliefs and the observed data.

Note that the HDI does not necessarily have to be symmetric around the peak
of the distribution.

There could be, for instance, two peaks in the distribution, as you can see
\href{https://bookdown.org/content/3686/04_files/figure-html/unnamed-chunk-30-1.png}{here} for instance.
In that case, the HDI would consist of two intervals. One could readily image
a real life case for such a distribution:

Think of a group of people where the
measurement of interest is the 100 m sprint time. There could be two groups of people:
The hobby runners and the professional athletes. The distribution of the sprint times would
probably be bimodal showing two peaks for the two groups.

There are other ways, one can construct credible intervals.
We could also use the quantiles of the posterior distribution to construct a credible interval.
If the distribution is symmetric, the quantiles are symmetric around the peak of the distribution
and the HDI is the same as the quantile-based credible interval.

A \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Credible_interval}{credible interval}
is \textbf{not} the same as a
\href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Confidence_interval}{confidence interval} in Frequentist statistics.
We will discuss this in the next chapter.

Briefly, a \textbf{credible interval} is a range of values
for a parameter of interest that has a specified probability of containing the
unobserved parameter.

A \textbf{confidence interval} is an interval which is expected to contain the \textbf{true,
but unknown} parameter of interest in a certain percent of times (e.g., 92\%), when
\href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Confidence_interval\#/media/File:Normal_distribution_50\%25_CI_illustration.svg}{constructed \textbf{repeatedly} every time a new sample is drawn}.

\section{\texorpdfstring{Bayesian \(t\)-test}{Bayesian t-test}}\label{bayesian_t_test}

Above, we looked at some aspects of estimating a parameter (proportion) using Bayes' theorem.
We can answer all sorts of other questions using this paradigm. In classical statistics,
one often wants to know if two groups differ with respect to their true means.
This answer is typically given by the famous \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Student\%27s_t-test}{\(t\)-test}
(\href{https://pure.manchester.ac.uk/ws/portalfiles/portal/78743532/studentt.pdf}{small ``\(t\)''} please).
I encourage you to read the short \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Student\%27s_t-test\#History}{history} of the \(t\)-test.

For didactic reasons, we will look at the Bayesian version of the \(t\)-test first.
We want to try to view statistics as more than just a
\href{https://www.methodenberatung.uzh.ch/de/datenanalyse_spss.html}{cookbook of recipes}.
Unfortunately, very often it is taught that way and the impression is given that statistics is
just a set of tools to apply in a certain order. In my humble opinion, this is not the case. Statistics
and model building is a creative process and - if you want to go so far - an art form.
\textbf{Statistics is difficult and beautiful - in that order}.

Unfortunately, it is not completely trivial to apply the Bayesian \(t\)-test,
as opposte to the Frequentist \(t\)-test,
which constitutes one line of code in R.

We'll use the not anymore maintained R package \href{https://cran.r-project.org/web/packages/BEST/index.html}{BEST}
for a nice illustration. Later, we will use more up-to-date packages like \href{https://cran.r-project.org/web/packages/brms/index.html}{brms}
or \href{https://github.com/rmcelreath/rethinking}{rethinking} for modeling.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Example - Bayesian \(t\)-test}{Example - Bayesian t-test}}\label{example---bayesian-t-test}

For an explanation of the statistical model behind the Bayesian \(t\)-test,
visit \href{https://www.krigolsonteaching.com/uploads/4/3/8/4/43848243/kruschke2012jepg.pdf}{Kruschke's paper}, Figure 2.

\begin{Shaded}

\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

We work with a rather small sample size, 8 in each group.
\textbf{We want to know if the two groups differ in their (unobserved) means}.
For this, we will apply the R function \texttt{BESTmcmc} from the package \texttt{BEST}.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{set.seed}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{33443}\NormalTok{)}
\FunctionTok{p\_load}\NormalTok{(HDInterval, BEST, tictoc, psych, tidyverse)}
\CommentTok{\# H\_0: mue1 {-} mue2 = delta\_0}
\NormalTok{a }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \DecValTok{20} \CommentTok{\# ROPE (region of practical equivalence)}
\NormalTok{d\_0 }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \DecValTok{0}
\NormalTok{BESTout }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{BESTmcmc}\NormalTok{(y1, y2, }\AttributeTok{verbose =} \ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Waiting for parallel processing to complete...done.
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{plot}\NormalTok{(BESTout, }\AttributeTok{which =} \StringTok{"mean"}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{compVal =}\NormalTok{ d\_0,}
     \AttributeTok{ROPE =}\NormalTok{ d\_0 }\SpecialCharTok{+} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{*}\NormalTok{ a, }\AttributeTok{showCurve =} \ConstantTok{FALSE}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{credMass =} \FloatTok{0.93}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{_main_files/_main_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-43-1.pdf}}

\texttt{BESTmcmc} is a function that uses a \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Markov_chain_Monte_Carlo}{Markov Chain Monte Carlo (MCMC)}
algorithm to estimate the \textbf{posterior distribution of the difference between the means of two groups}
(the above is not just a histogram of differences).
We do not care about these details for now, but try to interpret what we see.

The plot shows the posterior distribution of the difference between the means of the two groups.

\begin{itemize}
\item
  With the parameter \texttt{which\ =\ "mean"}, we are interested in the \textbf{posterior distribution of
  difference of the means} (\(\mu_1 - \mu_2\)). We see a full probability distribution again.
\item
  The parameter \texttt{compVal\ =\ d\_0} is the value we want to compare the posterior distribution to. Here We
  chose delta\_0 = 0, which means that we want to know if the difference between the means is
  different from zero.
\item
  The parameter \texttt{ROPE\ =\ d\_0\ +\ c(-1,\ 1)\ *\ a} (\(= -20\) to \(20\)) defines the region of practical equivalence (ROPE).
  In our case, we chose a = 20. This means that we consider differences between the means of the two groups
  of \(\pm 20\) as practically equivalent. In practice, \textbf{you would choose a value that is meaningful for your
  particular research question}. For instance, in a planned experiment, we are interested in changes
  on the Roland Morris Disability Questionnaire (RMDQ) of 2 points.
  We would then choose a value of 2 for the ROPE, since only a change beyond this value would be
  clinically relevant for the patient.
\item
  The parameter \texttt{credMass\ =\ 0.93} is the probability mass to include in credible intervals, in this case 93\%.
\item
  The 93\% HDI for the difference between the means is shown in the graph, hence given
  the prior information and the observed data, we can be 93\% sure that the
  difference between the means is in this interval. The HDI changes every time the code of this
  script is run since it's simulation based.
\end{itemize}

We can do more:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{summary}\NormalTok{(BESTout, }\AttributeTok{ROPEm =}\NormalTok{ d\_0 }\SpecialCharTok{+} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{*}\NormalTok{ a, }\AttributeTok{compValm =} \DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{,}
        \AttributeTok{digits =} \DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{credMass =} \FloatTok{0.93}\NormalTok{) }\CommentTok{\# summary.BEST()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##             mean  median    mode HDI%     HDIlo  HDIup compVal %>compVal
## mu1       131.18 131.179 130.557   93   34.2090 227.26                  
## mu2         5.95   5.672   6.051   93  -84.6159  97.25                  
## muDiff    125.23 125.496 125.251   93   -9.0164 259.44       0      95.6
## sigma1    139.16 128.478 116.287   93   66.1289 224.14                  
## sigma2    130.79 119.452 106.217   93   60.8465 213.15                  
## sigmaDiff   8.37   8.725   9.627   93 -122.6760 134.89       0      56.5
## nu         34.32  25.560   9.512   93    1.1621  83.67                  
## log10nu     1.38   1.408   1.482   93    0.6378   2.04                  
## effSz       0.97   0.958   0.911   93   -0.0863   2.02       0      95.6
##           ROPElow ROPEhigh %InROPE
## mu1                               
## mu2                               
## muDiff        -20       20    4.51
## sigma1                            
## sigma2                            
## sigmaDiff                         
## nu                                
## log10nu                           
## effSz
\end{verbatim}

If you look at the row ``muDiff'' and the column ``\%\textgreater compVal''. This is the percentage
of the posterior distribution that is greater than 0. We can say, with a probability of
\textasciitilde95\%, the difference between the means is greater than 0. This is nice but not the whole story.
The true difference can by greater than 0, but still rather small and clinically irrelevant.

Would we conclude that the two groups differ in their means?
According to \href{https://nyu-cdsc.github.io/learningr/assets/kruschke_bayesian_in_R.pdf}{Kruschke} (p.336):

\textbf{``A parameter value is declared to be not credible,
or rejected, if its entire ROPE lies outside the highest density interval
(HDI) of the posterior distribution of that parameter.''}

This is not the case here. We would not conclude, that the means between the groups differ.
One could argue, that this rule seems rather strict considering, that \textasciitilde95\% of the
posterior distribution of the differences is greater than 0.

As opposed to the Frequentist \(t\)-test, we can also \textbf{affirm the null hypothesis},
that the means in the two groups are equal. This is a nice feature of the Bayesian \(t\)-test.

\textbf{``A parameter value is declared to be accepted for practical purposes if that value's
ROPE completely contains the X\% HDI of the posterior of that parameter.''}

This is also not the case here. The 93\% HDI lies outside the ROPE to a large part.

Note, ``The decision rule for accepting the null value says merely that the most
credible values are practically equivalent to the null value according to the
chosen ROPE, not necessarily that the null value has high credibility.''
(\href{https://nyu-cdsc.github.io/learningr/assets/kruschke_bayesian_in_R.pdf}{Kruschke}, p.337)

We are above in the situation, that we cannot make a clear decision.
The ROPE does not fully contain the 93\% HDI, and the 93\% HDI is not
completely outside the ROPE.

\textbf{``When the HDI and ROPE overlap, with the ROPE not completely
containing the HDI, then neither of the above decision rules is satisfied,
and we withhold a decision. This means merely that the current data are insufficient to
yield a clear decision one way or the other, according to the stated decision criteria.''}
(\href{https://nyu-cdsc.github.io/learningr/assets/kruschke_bayesian_in_R.pdf}{Kruschke}, p.337)''

What have we learned so far:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Estimating a proportion using Bayes' theorem \hyperref[example_defective_products]{Example about the proportion of defective products}
\item
  Using a whole continuous distribution as prior knowledge \hyperref[example_defective_products_extended]{Example about the proportion of defective products extended}
\item
  Analysis if two groups differ in their means or have the same mean \hyperref[bayesian_t_test]{Example about the Bayesian t-test}
\end{itemize}

Latest in QM2, we will do Bayesian regression analysis using packages like \texttt{brms} from
\href{https://www.tu-dortmund.de/universitaet/neuberufene-professorinnen/prof-paul-christian-buerkner/}{Paul Bürkner} or
\texttt{rethinking} from \href{https://www.eva.mpg.de/ecology/staff/richard-mcelreath/}{Richard McElreath}.

\section{Bayesian updating}\label{bayesian-updating}

See also \emph{5.2.1. Data-order invariance} in \href{https://nyu-cdsc.github.io/learningr/assets/kruschke_bayesian_in_R.pdf}{Kruschke}.

The following is an animated example of Bayesian updating.
The true probability for heads in a coin toss is \(\theta = 0.77\).
We throw a coin 100 times and we start with a uniform prior distribution, i.e.,
every value between 0 and 1 is equally likely at the beginning.
Every time a coin is tossed, the prior distribution is updated with the new data.
The posterior distribution is the prior for the next coin toss et cetera.
One can see that we are converging to the ``truth'' (which is normally not known).

Code can be found \href{https://github.com/jdegenfellner/Script_QM1_ZHAW/blob/main/R_codes_with_long_compute_time/posterior.R}{here.}

\begin{verbatim}
## Linking to ImageMagick 6.9.13.29
## Enabled features: cairo, fontconfig, freetype, heic, lcms, pango, raw, rsvg, webp
## Disabled features: fftw, ghostscript, x11
\end{verbatim}

\begin{figure}
\includegraphics[width=6.67in]{_main_files/figure-latex/testing-1} \caption{Bayesian updating: the posterior converges towards the true value as more coin tosses are observed.}\label{fig:testing}
\end{figure}

This \href{https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=rUoJvogN7qQ&t=4s&ab_channel=CoreyChivers}{video} illustrates the concept of Bayesian updating as well
using two different starting points. The opinions seem to converge.

\section{More complex parameter spaces}\label{more-complex-parameter-spaces}

Above we dealt with the rather simple case of one parameter (\(\theta\)), the proportion of defective products or the fairness of a coin.
Some of these cases can be solved analytically, like the coin toss example. One can calculate the shape of the posterior distribution
exactly. \textbf{We are mainly interested in simulation-based results, since we are practitioners}.

In the Bayesian \(t\)-test, we had two groups and the difference of their means. Behind the scenes there were already more than two parameters
estimated (see Figure 2 \href{https://www.krigolsonteaching.com/uploads/4/3/8/4/43848243/kruschke2012jepg.pdf}{here}).
Here lies the computational bottleneck of Bayesian statistics. The more parameters we have, the more complex the parameter space becomes.
In order to estimate the posterior distribution, we have to sample from the parameter space by walking through intelligently.
This can be computationally intensive.
The animation in \href{https://mbjoseph.github.io/posts/2018-12-25-animating-the-metropolis-algorithm/}{this article}
shows the walk through of the parameter space nicely for the case of a normal distribution with two parameters (\(\mu\) and \(\sigma\)).

For statistical models with many parameters and observations, the computation can take a long time. Very often though, computational time
is not an issue and we can make use of the flexibility and intuitive interpretation of Bayesian statistics.

\section{Advantages/disadvantages of Bayesian statistics}\label{advantagesdisadvantages-of-bayesian-statistics}

\subsection{(Some) Advantages}\label{some-advantages}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Full probability distributions}: We get a full probability distribution for the parameter of interest.
\item
  \textbf{Flexibility}: We can incorporate prior knowledge into our analysis.
\item
  \textbf{Interpretability}: We can make statements about the probability of certain parameter values.
\item
  \textbf{No \(p\)-values}: We do not need to rely on \(p\)-values and NHST (Null Hypothesis Significance Testing).
  John Kruschke points out many problems with
  NHST in his \href{https://nyu-cdsc.github.io/learningr/assets/kruschke_bayesian_in_R.pdf}{book} and
  \href{https://www.krigolsonteaching.com/uploads/4/3/8/4/43848243/kruschke2012jepg.pdf}{article}. It seems that for many practinioners, \(p\)-values are
  hard to understand and interpret correctly. There are many
  \href{https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0037196308000620}{misconceptions about \(p\)-values}.
  And one should definitely
  \href{https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/00031305.2019.1583913}{move away from dichotomous thinking of ``significant'' and ``non-significant''} results.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{(Some) Disadvantages}\label{some-disadvantages}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Computational complexity}: Calculating the posterior distribution can be computationally intensive.
  Even with modern computers and the latest packages, it can take a long time to get results. Classical statistical models
  are often estimated within fractions of a second.
\item
  \textbf{Barriers to entry}: Statistics courses and textbooks often focus on Frequentist statistics, as well as many
  software packages lean more towards Frequentist statistics. This can make it difficult for newcomers to learn Bayesian statistics.
  When publishing, many reviewers could be sceptical or unfamiliar with Bayesian statistics.
\item
  \textbf{Subjectivity}: The choice of the prior distribution can be subjective.
\end{itemize}

\section{Exercises}\label{exercises-2}

Difficulty levels of exercises:
\textbf{E}: easy,
\textbf{M}: intermediate,
\textbf{D}: difficult

\subsection{{[}E{]} Exercise 1 - defective product rate}\label{exercise_defective_product_rate}

Let's revisit \hyperref[example_defective_products]{Example 1} and change the prior probabilities to
\(\pi(0.05) = 0.1\) and \(\pi(0.10) = 0.9\).

Calculate and plot the posterior probabilities for \(\theta = 0.05\) and \(\theta = 0.10\).

\subsection{{[}H{]} Exercise 2 - Bayesian updating}\label{h-exercise-2---bayesian-updating}

We want to check empirically that the \textbf{order of data collection does not influence the posterior distribution}.
We want to find out the probability \(\theta\) of a therapy to work.
Since we have absolutely no idea, how effective the therapy is, let our prior distribution be a \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continuous_uniform_distribution}{uniform
distribution} between 0 and 1
(this has to be argued for more rigorously in practice). Hence,
every value between 0 and 1 is equally likely:

\(\pi(\theta) = 1\) for \(\theta \in [0,1]\).

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Load required libraries}
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(ggplot2)}

\CommentTok{\# Generate data for a uniform distribution}
\NormalTok{theta }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{seq}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{length.out =} \DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{)  }\CommentTok{\# Range of theta}

\CommentTok{\# Create a data frame for ggplot}
\NormalTok{df }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{theta =}\NormalTok{ theta, }\AttributeTok{density =}\NormalTok{ density)}

\CommentTok{\# Plot the density function}
\FunctionTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(df, }\FunctionTok{aes}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{x =}\NormalTok{ theta, }\AttributeTok{y =}\NormalTok{ density)) }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \FunctionTok{geom\_line}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{color =} \StringTok{"blue"}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{linewidth =} \FloatTok{1.2}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{+}  \CommentTok{\# Density line}

  \FunctionTok{labs}\NormalTok{(}
    \AttributeTok{title =} \StringTok{"Prior: Uniform Distribution"}\NormalTok{,}

\NormalTok{  ) }\SpecialCharTok{+}

\end{Highlighting}

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{_main_files/_main_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-45-1.pdf}}

Notize, that the area under the curve is 1, since it is a probability density function.
For example, \(\mathbb{P}(\theta \in (0.25,0.75)) = 0.5\)

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Experiment 1 one shows that the therapy works in 17 out of 50 cases.
\item
  Experiment 2 shows that the therapy works in 23 out of 50 cases.
\end{itemize}

So we go from:

Prior \(\rightarrow\) experiment 1 \(\rightarrow\) posterior 1 \(\rightarrow\) experiment 2 \(\rightarrow\) posterior12.

Or

Prior \(\rightarrow\) experiment 2 \(\rightarrow\) posterior 2 \(\rightarrow\) experiment 1 \(\rightarrow\) posterior21.

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Show that posterior12 and posterior21 should be the same and draw both posterior distributions into one plot.
\item
  {[}Optional{]} Show this result analytically.
\end{itemize}

sleep: Daten, die die Wirkung von zwei schlaffördernden Medikamenten
(Zunahme der Schlafstunden im Vergleich zur Kontrollgruppe) bei 10 Patienten zeigen.

Wir führen einen Bayesian \(t\)-test auf Gleichheit der Mittelwerte der beiden Gruppen durch:

Welche der folgenden Aussage(n) ist/sind korrekt (0-4 korrekte Antworten)?

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  -1.59 ist eine (aufgrund des Bayes \(t\)-tests) plausible Schätzung für die
  Differenz der Mittelwerte der beiden Gruppen.
\item
  Wäre die ROPE halb so groß, würde die Testentscheidung anders ausfallen.
\item
  93\% der posterior Verteilung der Differenz der Mittelwerte liegt
  im Interval \([-3.48, 0.298]\).
\item
  Die Testentscheidung lautet: ``Die Mittelwerte der beiden Gruppen sind nicht gleich.''
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Frage 2}\label{frage-2}

\[
\mathbb{P}(Dpos|Tpos) = \frac{\mathbb{P}(Tpos|Dpos) \cdot 
\mathbb{P}(Dpos)}{\mathbb{P}(Tpos|Dpos) \cdot \mathbb{P}(Dpos) + (1-\mathbb{P}(Tneg|Dneg)) \cdot \mathbb{P}(Dneg)}.
\]

Welche der folgenden Aussagen ist/sind korrekt (0-4 korrekte Antworten)?

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Mit einer Spezifität von 1, wäre die Wahrscheinlichkeit für eine Krankheit,
  wenn der Test positiv ist, gleich 1.
\item
  Mit einer Sensitivität von 1, wäre die Wahrscheinlichkeit für eine Krankheit,
  wenn der Test positiv ist, gleich 1.
\item
  Falls \(\mathbb{P}(Dpos) = 1\), ist \(\mathbb{P}(Dpos|Tpos) = 1\).
\item
  Im Nenner des Bayes'schen Theorems steht die Wahrscheinlichkeit, dass der Test
  positiv ist.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Frage 3}\label{frage-3}

Bei einer Bayes'schen Analyse eines Fehleranteils verwenden wir folgende a
priori Verteilung für den Fehleranteil \(\theta\):

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{_main_files/_main_files/figure-latex/bar_plot_code_ueb-1.pdf}}

Welche der folgenden Aussagen ist/sind korrekt (0-4 korrekte Antworten)?

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Die a priori Wahrscheinlichkeit, dass der Fehleranteil \(0.02\) beträgt, ist \(0.02\).
\item
  Die a priori Wahrscheinlichkeit, dass der Fehleranteil im (geschlossenen) Interval \([0.02, 0.08]\) liegt, ist 0.
\item
  Hier handelt es sich um eine diskrete a priori Verteilung und man untersucht lediglich, welcher
  der beiden Fehleranteile wahrscheinlicher ist.
\item
  Man beobachtet bei einer Stichprobe von \(20\) Produkten, dass \(0\) davon fehlerhaft sind.
  A posteriori wird der Fehleranteil \(0.08\) nun mehr als 50\% Wahrscheinlichkeit erhalten.
\end{itemize}

\chapter{Null Hypothesis Significance Testing (NHST)}\label{nhst}

Null Hypothesis Significance Testing (NHST) is a statistical method widely used in research,
including health sciences, to evaluate whether observed data provide sufficient evidence \textbf{to refute} a specific hypothesis.
It operates within a framework of probability and decision-making to address the following question:

\textbf{Are the observed results likely to occur by chance alone if the null hypothesis is true (and other model assumptions are not strongly violated)?}

If one decides that the observed results are unlikely to occur by chance alone,
the null hypothesis is rejected in favor of an alternative hypothesis.

NHST is \textbf{not designed to ``prove'' hypotheses} but rather to provide evidence against the null hypothesis.
If one reads in a paper that there was ``no association'', this typically means the
\(p\)-value was larger than the arbitrary threshold of \(0.05\). This does \emph{not} imply that
there is no association (Misconception 2 \href{https://sixsigmadsi.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/10/A-Dirty-Dozen-Twelve-P-Value-Misconceptions.pdf}{here}).

Underlying NHST is the idea of \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Falsifiability}{falsifiability}.
Sometimes one counter example is enough to reject a hypothesis like ``all swans are white''.
Seeing one \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Falsifiability\#/media/File:Black_Swans.jpg}{black swan}
proves the hypothesis wrong.

\textbf{Key concepts}:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Null Hypothesis \(H_0\)}: This represents the assumption of some specific effect or no effect at all.
  For example, in a clinical trial comparing two treatments, one might state that both treatments have the same effect.
  The alternative is that one treatment is \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clinical_study_design\#Other_terms}{superior to the other}.
\item
  \textbf{Alternative Hypothesis \(H_1\)}: This is the opposing claim to \(H_0\), the logical complement.
  Example:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \(H_0: \theta \le 0.4\)
  \item
    \(H_1: \theta > 0.4\)
  \item
    \(H_0\) states that the treatment effect is less than or equal to 0.4,
    while \(H_1\) states that the treatment effect is greater than 0.4.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{\(p\)-value}: The \(p\)-value quantifies the probability of obtaining results (test statistic) as extreme as (or more extreme than)
  the observed data, assuming that \(H_0\) is true.
  A smaller \(p\)-value indicates stronger evidence against \(H_0\).
  There are many \href{https://sixsigmadsi.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/10/A-Dirty-Dozen-Twelve-P-Value-Misconceptions.pdf}{misconeptions about \(p\)-values}.
\item
  \textbf{Significance level \(\alpha\)}: Researchers set a threshold to determine whether to reject \(H_0\).
  If the \(p\)-value is smaller than \(\alpha\), \(H_0\) is rejected in favor of \(H_1\).
  Note that \textbf{there is absolutely no special reason to use \(\alpha = 0.05\) as a default value}.
  To quote \href{https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-1-4612-4380-9_6}{Ronald Fisher} (p.44):
  ``The value for which P = .05, or 1 in 20, is 1.96 or nearly 2; it is convenient to take this
  point as a limit in judging whether a deviation is to be considered significant or not.''
\end{itemize}

\textbf{How NHST works}:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Formulate Hypotheses: Define \(H_0\) (e.g., ``The new therapy has no effect'')
  and \(H_1\) (e.g., ``The new therapy improves outcomes'').
\item
  Determine the necessary sample size to find the effect.
\item
  Collect data: Perform an experiment or study to gather relevant data.
\item
  Calculate the test statistic: Compute a value based on the sample data that
  reflects the difference or effect under investigation.
\item
  Compute the \(p\)-value: Determine the probability of observing the
  test statistic (or more extreme values) if \(H_0\) is true.
\item
  Make a decision: Compare the \(p\)-value to the significance level:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    If \(p < \alpha\): Reject \(H_0\); evidence suggests \(H_1\) is true.
  \item
    If \(p \ge \alpha\): Fail to reject \(H_0\); insufficient evidence to support \(H_1\).
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\textbf{(Some) Limitations of NHST}:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Focus on \(p\)-values with hard cut-offs}: Solely relying on \(p\)-values can lead to overinterpretation of
  results without considering practical significance.
\item
  \textbf{Dichotomous thinking}: The decision to ``reject'' or ``fail to reject'' \(H_0\)
  oversimplifies the complexity of real-world data. This binary thinking
  incentivizes researchers to \href{https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/00031305.2018.1447512}{focus on statistical significance}
  rather than real relevance. It happened to me not only once that a colleague
  looked at me with a sad facial expression announcing that the \(p\)-value was ``not significant''.
  This should be not an issue at all. The focus should be on doing useful analyses in the most
  rigorous way possible. Note that the \href{https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1198/000313006x152649?casa_token=mn4mIBOnoaIAAAAA:RmZ3RZ6prFHNPWte07aNMKidAsQGmqsjPdCQCpXraH8MCmFuftZmXTsLNzeSLFmXdgJm0Xd8dklEKZdPUw}{difference between ``significant'' and ``not significant'' is
  itself not significant}.
\item
  \textbf{Sample size influence}: Large samples can make small, clinically irrelevant
  differences ``statistically significant''. Example: Given an arbitrarily small difference between means of two groups. There is
  always a sample size that makes the difference ``significant''. See \hyperref[exercise2_nhst]{Exercise 2}.
\item
  \textbf{\href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Publication_bias}{Publication bias}}: Many journals tended to publish studies with ``significant'' results. ``Not significant'' results
  were often not published. This leads to a distorted view of the literature. All rigorously
  conducted studies should be published, irrespective of the results. Estimates of parameters will also be \textbf{overestimated}
  when focusing on ``significant'' results only.
\item
  \textbf{\href{https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/P-Hacking}{\(p\)-hacking}}: In the pursuit for ``significant'' results
  (which results in publications, which results in tenure), it is natural to do everything to get them.
  This can include data dredging, selective reporting, and other questionable practices.
  In my humble opinion, very often, researchers are not to blame but incentives. It is important to understand
  that probabilities are defined \emph{before} an event has happened. The probability for the esteemed reader to win the
  Swiss lottery is rather small and I would take a large bet against it. But every week, someone wins (approximately).
  After the fact, one should not be surprised \emph{that} someone won,
  since it follows from the \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Law_of_large_numbers}{law of large numbers}.
\end{itemize}

See \href{https://www.krigolsonteaching.com/uploads/4/3/8/4/43848243/kruschke2012jepg.pdf}{Kruschke} for more limitations of NHST.

In practice, NHST should at last be accompanied by confidence intervals,
effect size calculations, and a focus on clinical relevance to provide
a more comprehensive understanding of the results.

This \href{https://f1000research.com/articles/4-621/v3}{Review of NHST} or
\href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Statistical_hypothesis_test}{this overview} might be a good entry point.

This \href{https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=FZ2_hzMyJpY&ab_channel=VeryNormal}{video} could also help to understand the basic concepts.

\section{Example in the literature}\label{example-in-the-literature}

\(p\)-values are omnipresent in the scientific literature. There rather few papers in our field that do not contain them.
We \textbf{do not use \(p\)-values in descriptive tables}
(see \href{https://epitodate.com/the-balance-test-fallacy-why-you-shouldnt-put-p-values-in-table-1/}{here} and
\href{https://www.acpjournals.org/doi/10.7326/0003-4819-147-8-200710160-00010-w1}{here}).

\href{https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/09593980601023754\#d1e100}{Here} is an example from the literature.
Table 2 lists studies and one column contains \(p\)-values. Note, that it is not good practice to present \(p\)-values
(if one should use them at all) as dichotomy: \(p < 0.05\)
(Misconception 8 \href{https://sixsigmadsi.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/10/A-Dirty-Dozen-Twelve-P-Value-Misconceptions.pdf}{here}). This statement does
not allow to judge the strength of the evidence against the null hypothesis. Both, \(p = 0.00000001\) and \(p = 0.049\) would satisfy
the inequality. Since this dichotomy decides around an arbitrary threshold (\(\alpha = 0.05\)),
the decision is also somewhat arbitrary.

When reading papers, watch out for oceans of \(p\)-values and their selective dichotomous interpretation. This is a clear warning sign.

\section{Binomial test}\label{binomial-test}

In the first chapter, we invented the 1000-researcher experiment. There, we have already encountered hypothesis tests in disguise.
If we would assume that the probability of a false positive is 0.04,
we would ``expect'' (around) 40 false positives. We asked, what is the probability of observing
137 or more. This is an example of a one-sided hypothesis test:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(H_0: \theta \le 0.04\)
\item
  \(H_1: \theta > 0.04\)
\end{itemize}

Under \(H_0\), what is the probability to see the observed number (137) of
false positives (in our case, this is the test statistic) or more?

The answer was \(p = 5.551115 \cdot 10^{-16}\) (or less if one chooses \(\theta < 0.04\)).

A reasonable person would say, that this result did not happen by chance alone and
therefore conclude, the true, but unknown false positive rate \(\theta\) is larger than 0.04.

Formally, this is called a \textbf{(one-sided) \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binomial_test}{binomial test}}.

Note, that \textbf{\(H_1\) is the logical complement of \(H_0\)}.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# binomial test}
\FunctionTok{binom.test}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{137}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1000}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{p =} \FloatTok{0.04}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{alternative =} \StringTok{"greater"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Exact binomial test
## 
## data:  137 and 1000
## number of successes = 137, number of trials = 1000, p-value < 2.2e-16
## alternative hypothesis: true probability of success is greater than 0.04
## 95 percent confidence interval:
##  0.1194241 1.0000000
## sample estimates:
## probability of success 
##                  0.137
\end{verbatim}

The output in R tells us the following:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  data: 137 and 1000 successes observed
\item
  alternative hypothesis: true probability of success is greater than 0.04, which we assume afterwards.
\item
  \(p\)-value \textless{} \(2.2e-16\). This value is smaller than the precision in R (`.Machine').
\item
  95 percent confidence interval: \(0.1194241\) to \(1.0000000\).
  The upper limit of \(1\) occurs since we have a one-sided test.
  95\% is just convention and has no special meaning.
\item
  Sample estimates: Estimating the true (but unknown) proportion from the sample would just be:
  \(\frac{137}{1000} = 0.137\)
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Two-sided test}:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(H_0: \theta = 0.04\)
\item
  \(H_1: \theta \ne 0.04\)
\end{itemize}

One could argue that this is bad style, since we should probably know the direction of the effect
(see also 4.2. \href{https://errorstatistics.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/07/cox-1977-with-discussion-and-reply-nc.pdf}{here}).

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{binom.test}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{137}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1000}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{p =} \FloatTok{0.04}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{alternative =} \StringTok{"two.sided"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Exact binomial test
## 
## data:  137 and 1000
## number of successes = 137, number of trials = 1000, p-value < 2.2e-16
## alternative hypothesis: true probability of success is not equal to 0.04
## 95 percent confidence interval:
##  0.1162817 0.1598810
## sample estimates:
## probability of success 
##                  0.137
\end{verbatim}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  alternative = ``two.sided'', this indicates that we are interested in both directions (higher or lower than \(0.04\)).
  With some experience, one would probably not test for lower when seeing the observed number of 137.
\item
  95 percent confidence interval: \(0.1162817\) to \(0.1598810\).
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Interpretation of this frequentist confidence interval (CI)}: When drawing repeated samples, in 95\% percent of the samples,
the so constructed interval (which will be different every time) contains the true but unknown
parameter (see Illustration \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Confidence_interval\#/media/File:Normal_distribution_50\%25_CI_illustration.svg}{here},
animations of the frequentist nature \href{https://seeing-theory.brown.edu/frequentist-inference/index.html}{here}).

Note, that the ``Exact binomial test'' was used. There were no approximations made. I would recommend always
using \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exact_test}{exact tests} if available, since we are in the 21th century and computers are fast.

Again, \textbf{the \(\alpha\) level of \(0.05\) has nothing special (apart from convention) to it}.
We can also use a \(\alpha = 0.14\) level. In this case, we construct confidence intervals
with a 86\% confidence level. We'll discuss the \(\alpha\) level \hyperref[error_types]{below}.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{binom.test}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{137}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1000}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{p =} \FloatTok{0.04}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{alternative =} \StringTok{"two.sided"}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{conf.level =} \FloatTok{0.86}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Exact binomial test
## 
## data:  137 and 1000
## number of successes = 137, number of trials = 1000, p-value < 2.2e-16
## alternative hypothesis: true probability of success is not equal to 0.04
## 86 percent confidence interval:
##  0.1211304 0.1542134
## sample estimates:
## probability of success 
##                  0.137
\end{verbatim}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  confidence interval: \(0.1211304\) to \(0.1542134\).
\end{itemize}

With \textbf{smaller coverage probability} (86 instead of 95), we get a \textbf{narrower interval}.
Trivially, a 100\% confidence interval would be \(0\) to \(1\) and a 0\% confidence interval would be \(0.04\) to \(0.04\) or any other
specific value assuming that the true parameter can take any value from \(0\) to \(1\).

See also \hyperref[exercise3_nhst]{Exercise 3}.

\textbf{Comparison with Bayesian version} of estimating \(\theta\):

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  We cannot include a prior distribution for the parameter \(\theta\).
\item
  We cannot calculate the posterior distribution of \(\theta\).
  Hence, we cannot make statements like ``the probability that \(\theta\) is larger than 0.04 is 0.9''.
\item
  Prior knowledge could probably be included in the form of the null hypothesis stating, for instance, that \(\theta \le 0.2\).
  This could be based on previous studies or expert knowledge.
\end{itemize}

\section{Proportions test}\label{proportions-test}

If we are interested in comparing proportions,
we can use the \href{https://www.rdocumentation.org/packages/stats/versions/3.6.2/topics/prop.test}{proportions test}.

\subsection{One sample case}\label{one-sample-case}

\(H_0: p = 0.5\). The true, but unknown proportion is 0.5.

\(H_1: p \ne 0.5\). The true proportion is different from 0.5.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{set.seed}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{443}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{heads }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{rbinom}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{size =} \DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{,}
                \AttributeTok{prob =} \FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{) }\CommentTok{\# create a sample with known probability}
\FunctionTok{prop.test}\NormalTok{(heads, }\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{,}
          \AttributeTok{conf.level =} \FloatTok{0.94}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{p =} \FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{) }\CommentTok{\# continuity correction TRUE by default}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  1-sample proportions test with continuity correction
## 
## data:  heads out of 100, null probability 0.5
## X-squared = 0.25, df = 1, p-value = 0.6171
## alternative hypothesis: true p is not equal to 0.5
## 94 percent confidence interval:
##  0.3739955 0.5681618
## sample estimates:
##    p 
## 0.47
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{prop.test}\NormalTok{(heads, }\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{,}
          \AttributeTok{correct =} \ConstantTok{FALSE}\NormalTok{,}
          \AttributeTok{conf.level =} \FloatTok{0.94}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{p =} \FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{) }\CommentTok{\# no continuity correction}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  1-sample proportions test without continuity correction
## 
## data:  heads out of 100, null probability 0.5
## X-squared = 0.36, df = 1, p-value = 0.5485
## alternative hypothesis: true p is not equal to 0.5
## 94 percent confidence interval:
##  0.3787665 0.5632834
## sample estimates:
##    p 
## 0.47
\end{verbatim}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  correct = TRUE indicates that a correction (\href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yates\%27s_correction_for_continuity}{Yates})
  is used to make the test more accurate and consider the fact that the test statistic is in fact discrete.
\item
  94\% confidence interval: \(0.3739955\) to \(0.5681618\).
\item
  \(\chi^2 = 0.25\). This is the value of the test statistic used in the test.
  Under the null hypothesis (in this case \(H_0: p = 0.5\)),
  the test statistic follows a \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chi-squared_distribution}{\(\chi^2\) distribution}.
\end{itemize}

See also \hyperref[exercise4_nhst]{Exercise 4}.

\textbf{Let's verify the test statistic for the test without continuity correction by ``hand'':}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Observed and expected values}
\NormalTok{heads }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \DecValTok{47}  \CommentTok{\# Observed count of heads}
\NormalTok{total }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \DecValTok{100}  \CommentTok{\# Total flips}
\NormalTok{p\_null }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FloatTok{0.5}  \CommentTok{\# Null hypothesis proportion}

\CommentTok{\# Observed proportion}
\NormalTok{p\_observed }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\NormalTok{ heads }\SpecialCharTok{/}\NormalTok{ total}

\CommentTok{\# Z{-}test statistic}
\NormalTok{Z }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\NormalTok{ (p\_observed }\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ p\_null) }\SpecialCharTok{/} \FunctionTok{sqrt}\NormalTok{((p\_null }\SpecialCharTok{*}\NormalTok{ (}\DecValTok{1} \SpecialCharTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ p\_null)) }\SpecialCharTok{/}\NormalTok{ total)}

\CommentTok{\# Chi{-}squared test statistic}
\NormalTok{X\_squared }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\NormalTok{ Z}\SpecialCharTok{\^{}}\DecValTok{2}

\CommentTok{\# Print the results}
\NormalTok{Z}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] -0.6
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{X\_squared}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 0.36
\end{verbatim}

The \(\chi^2\) distribution can be \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chi-squared_distribution\#Definitions}{defined}
as the sum of squared standard normals \(Z\). See \hyperref[exercise8_nhst]{Exercise 8}.

\textbf{Where does the test statistic come from?}

According to the \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_limit_theorem}{central limit theorem},
\(\bar{X}\) (= the proportion of successes in the sample) is approximately normally distributed\\
\[\bar{X} = \hat{p} \sim N(p, \frac{\sigma^2}{n}) = N(p, \frac{p(1-p)}{n}).\]
This means, the statistic \(\hat{p}\) should show the pattern of a Gaußian distribution when repeated many times.
\(n\) is in the denominator, so the variance of the approximate normal distribution decreases with increasing sample size and we
can be more certain about the true proportion with increasing sample size.

Note that we want to find the true but unknown proportion \(p\) of a
\href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bernoulli_distribution}{Bernoulli distribution} which has expected value \(p\) and variance \(p(1-p)\).

Given this distributional statement, it follows that if we subtract the mean assumed under \(H_0\) (\(p_0 = 0.5\)) and divide by the standard deviation,
the resulting test statistic is (approximately) distributed according to a standard normal distribution:

\[Z = \frac{\hat{p} - p_0}{\sqrt{\frac{p_0(1-p_0)}{n}}} \sim N(0, 1).\]

This value squared gives us the value of the test statistic above: \(0.36\).

\subsubsection{Or the same with binomial test}\label{or-the-same-with-binomial-test}

\(H_0: p = 0.5\) The true but unknown proportion is \(0.5\).

\(H_1: p \ne 0.5\) The true proportion is different from \(0.5\).

This is a two-sided test.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{set.seed}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{443}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{heads }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{rbinom}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{size =} \DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{,}
                \AttributeTok{prob =} \FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{) }\CommentTok{\# create a sample with known probability}
\FunctionTok{binom.test}\NormalTok{(heads, }\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{,}
           \AttributeTok{conf.level =} \FloatTok{0.94}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{p =} \FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Exact binomial test
## 
## data:  heads and 100
## number of successes = 47, number of trials = 100, p-value = 0.6173
## alternative hypothesis: true probability of success is not equal to 0.5
## 94 percent confidence interval:
##  0.3731683 0.5685327
## sample estimates:
## probability of success 
##                   0.47
\end{verbatim}

We get a slightly different \(p\)-value and confidence interval but the same conclusion.

\textbf{Let's also verify the \(p\)-value and confidence interval by ``hand'':}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{\(p\)-value}: What is the probability that we get 47 successes
  or more ``extreme'' (into the direction of the \(H_1\)) assuming \(H_0\) is true?

  \(p\)-value \(=\mathbb{P}(\text{Number of successes} \ge 53 \text{ or} \le 47)\).

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{pbinom}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{47}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{ (}\DecValTok{1} \SpecialCharTok{{-}} \FunctionTok{pbinom}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{52}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 0.6172994
\end{verbatim}

  And the \(p\)-value is spot on.
\item
  \textbf{94\% Confidence interval}: These are the so-called exact \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binomial_proportion_confidence_interval\#Clopper\%E2\%80\%93Pearson_interval}{Clopper-Pearson intervals},
  since they always have coverage probability of at least \(1 - \alpha\).

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{binom.test}\NormalTok{(heads, }\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{conf.level =} \FloatTok{0.94}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{p =} \FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{)}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{conf.int}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 0.3731683 0.5685327
## attr(,"conf.level")
## [1] 0.94
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{pbinom}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{47}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{0.5685327}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 0.03000002
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\DecValTok{1}\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\FunctionTok{pbinom}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{46}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{0.3731683}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 0.02999993
\end{verbatim}

  The confidence interval is indeed \(0.3739955\) to \(0.5681618\).
\end{itemize}

\subsection{More than one proportion}\label{proportions_test_more_samples}

\(H_0: p_1 = p_2 = p_3 = p_4 = \frac{\sum smokers}{\sum patients} = 0.9370277\). Proportions are equal in all 4 groups.

\(H_1: \text{At least one of the proportions is different}\)

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(tidyverse)}
\CommentTok{\# Data from Fleiss (1981), p. 139.}
\CommentTok{\# H0: The null hypothesis is that the four populations from which}
\CommentTok{\#     the patients were drawn have the same true proportion of smokers.}
\CommentTok{\# H1: The alternative is that this proportion is different in at}
\CommentTok{\#     least one of the populations.}

\NormalTok{smokers  }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{83}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{90}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{129}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{70}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{patients }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{86}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{93}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{136}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{82}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{categories }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Group 1"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Group 2"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Group 3"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Group 4"}\NormalTok{)}

\FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(}
  \AttributeTok{Category =}\NormalTok{ categories,}
  \AttributeTok{Proportion =}\NormalTok{ smokers }\SpecialCharTok{/}\NormalTok{ patients}
\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}
  \FunctionTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{aes}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{x =}\NormalTok{ Category, }\AttributeTok{y =}\NormalTok{ Proportion)) }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \FunctionTok{geom\_bar}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{stat =} \StringTok{"identity"}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{fill =} \StringTok{"steelblue"}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{alpha =} \FloatTok{0.7}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \FunctionTok{labs}\NormalTok{(}
    \AttributeTok{title =} \StringTok{"Proportion of Smokers in Each Group"}\NormalTok{,}
    \AttributeTok{x =} \StringTok{""}\NormalTok{,}
    \AttributeTok{y =} \StringTok{"Proportion"}
\NormalTok{  ) }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \FunctionTok{ylim}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \FunctionTok{theme\_minimal}\NormalTok{() }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \FunctionTok{theme}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{plot.title =} \FunctionTok{element\_text}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{hjust =} \FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{_main_files/_main_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-55-1.pdf}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{prop.test}\NormalTok{(smokers, patients)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  4-sample test for equality of proportions without continuity correction
## 
## data:  smokers out of patients
## X-squared = 12.6, df = 3, p-value = 0.005585
## alternative hypothesis: two.sided
## sample estimates:
##    prop 1    prop 2    prop 3    prop 4 
## 0.9651163 0.9677419 0.9485294 0.8536585
\end{verbatim}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \(p\)-value \(= 0.005585\). One would argue that this test statistic is unlikely to have come about by chance
  alone and reject the null hypothesis, that all proportions are equal.
\item
  X-squared = 12.6, df = 3. The test statistic is distributed according to a \(\chi^2\) distribution
  with 3 \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Degrees_of_freedom_(statistics)}{degrees of freedom}.
  See also \hyperref[exercise5_nhst]{exercise 5}. Three degrees of freedom since we know the last component of the
  test statistic when 3 out of 4 are given.
  Degrees of freedom is the number of values in the final calculation of a statistic that are free to vary.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{The \(p\)-value is calculated quickly:}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{pchisq}\NormalTok{(}\FloatTok{12.6}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{df =} \DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{lower.tail =} \ConstantTok{FALSE}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 0.005586546
\end{verbatim}

\textbf{Let's verify the test statistic by ``hand''}:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{props }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\NormalTok{ smokers }\SpecialCharTok{/}\NormalTok{ patients}
\NormalTok{(p\_null }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{sum}\NormalTok{(smokers) }\SpecialCharTok{/} \FunctionTok{sum}\NormalTok{(patients))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 0.9370277
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{(Zs }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\NormalTok{ (props }\SpecialCharTok{{-}} \FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(p\_null, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{)) }\SpecialCharTok{/}\NormalTok{ (}\FunctionTok{sqrt}\NormalTok{(p\_null }\SpecialCharTok{*}\NormalTok{ (}\DecValTok{1} \SpecialCharTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ p\_null) }\SpecialCharTok{/}\NormalTok{ patients)))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1]  1.072329  1.219355  0.552180 -3.107860
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{Zs}\SpecialCharTok{\^{}}\DecValTok{2}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 1.1498887 1.4868262 0.3049028 9.6587936
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{sum}\NormalTok{(Zs}\SpecialCharTok{\^{}}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 12.60041
\end{verbatim}

We get exactly the same result by using
\href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pearson\%27s_chi-squared_test}{Pearson's chi-squared test}.

Formally, this is a \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chi-squared_test\#Test_of_independence}{chi-squared test for independence}.

\(H_0:\) Here, we test if the proportion of smokers is independent of the group.

If the \(H_0\) is true, the number of smokers/non-smokers (in each cell) is just determined
by the number of smokers/non-smokers
in the sample, how many people are in the respective group and the total number
of people in the sample.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{sm1 }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"smoke"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{83}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"nosmoke"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{sm2 }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"smoke"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{90}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"nosmoke"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{sm3 }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"smoke"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{129}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"nosmoke"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{7}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{sm4 }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"smoke"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{70}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"nosmoke"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{12}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{sm }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(sm1, sm2, sm3, sm4)}
\NormalTok{grp }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"A"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{86}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"B"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{93}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"C"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{136}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"D"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{82}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{d }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(sm, grp)}

\FunctionTok{table}\NormalTok{(d}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{sm, d}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{grp)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##          
##             A   B   C   D
##   nosmoke   3   3   7  12
##   smoke    83  90 129  70
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{chisq.test}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{table}\NormalTok{(d}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{sm, d}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{grp))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Pearson's Chi-squared test
## 
## data:  table(d$sm, d$grp)
## X-squared = 12.6, df = 3, p-value = 0.005585
\end{verbatim}

\textbf{Let's verify the test statistic by ``hand'':}

Degrees of freedom are:
\(df = (number\_of\_columns - 1) \cdot (number\_of\_rows - 1) = 3 \cdot 1 = 3\).

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Observed data}
\NormalTok{observed }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{matrix}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{83}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{90}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{7}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{129}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{12}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{70}\NormalTok{), }\AttributeTok{nrow =} \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{byrow =} \ConstantTok{FALSE}\NormalTok{)}
\FunctionTok{rownames}\NormalTok{(observed) }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"nosmoke"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"smoke"}\NormalTok{)}
\FunctionTok{colnames}\NormalTok{(observed) }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"A"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"B"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"C"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"D"}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Calculate row totals, column totals, and grand total}
\NormalTok{row\_totals }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{rowSums}\NormalTok{(observed)}
\NormalTok{col\_totals }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{colSums}\NormalTok{(observed)}
\NormalTok{grand\_total }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{sum}\NormalTok{(observed)}

\CommentTok{\# Calculate expected counts}
\NormalTok{expected }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{outer}\NormalTok{(row\_totals, col\_totals, }\AttributeTok{FUN =} \StringTok{"*"}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{/}\NormalTok{ grand\_total}

\FunctionTok{print}\NormalTok{(observed)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##          A  B   C  D
## nosmoke  3  3   7 12
## smoke   83 90 129 70
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{print}\NormalTok{(expected)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##                 A         B          C         D
## nosmoke  5.415617  5.856423   8.564232  5.163728
## smoke   80.584383 87.143577 127.435768 76.836272
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Chi{-}squared statistic}
\FunctionTok{sum}\NormalTok{((observed }\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ expected)}\SpecialCharTok{\^{}}\DecValTok{2} \SpecialCharTok{/}\NormalTok{ expected)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 12.60041
\end{verbatim}

For example, the first cell (nosmoke in group A) in the table is calculated as follows:
\(\frac{86 \cdot 25}{397} = \frac{86}{397} \cdot \frac{25}{397} \cdot 397 = 5.415617\).
These are the expected values in this cell,
if one would assume that the proportion of smokers does not depend on the group.
Hence, one just multiplies the column (\(86/397\)) and row (\(25/397\)) proportions
and multiples with the grand total (\(397\)) to get the absolute number of observations
in each cell under \(H_0\).

\subsection{Fisher's exact test}\label{fishers-exact-test}

As mentioned above, \textbf{we should always use exact tests if available}.
For the test for independence, we could also use
\href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fisher\%27s_exact_test}{Fisher's exact test}.
The underlying distribution for the test statistic is a hypergeometric distribution.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{fisher.test}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{table}\NormalTok{(d}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{sm, d}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{grp))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Fisher's Exact Test for Count Data
## 
## data:  table(d$sm, d$grp)
## p-value = 0.01447
## alternative hypothesis: two.sided
\end{verbatim}

\section{\texorpdfstring{(Classical) \(t\)-test}{(Classical) t-test}}\label{classical-t-test}

The \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Student\%27s_t-test}{\(t\)-test} is one of the most
famous classical statistical tests out there.
Consider these links: \href{https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=pXuFeRCMTAo&ab_channel=VeryNormal}{1}
\href{https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=pLNuDye_tq4&ab_channel=VeryNormal}{2}
as starting point.
\href{https://www.bmj.com/about-bmj/resources-readers/publications/statistics-square-one/7-t-tests}{This article} could also be interesting.

With the \(t\)-test, we want to answer the question

\begin{itemize}
\item
  if the true, but unobserved mean of a population is different from a
  specific value (\href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Student\%27s_t-test\#One-sample_t-test}{one sample \(t\)-test}) or
\item
  if the true, but unobserved means of two populations are different from each other (\href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Student\%27s_t-test\#Two-sample_t-tests}{two sample \(t\)-test}).

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Student\%27s_t-test\#Independent_two-sample_t-test}{independent samples}
  \item
    \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Student\%27s_t-test\#Dependent_t-test_for_paired_samples}{paired samples}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

Conveniently, R has a built-in function for these tests.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{One sample \(t\)-test}{One sample t-test}}\label{one-sample-t-test}

\(H_0: \mu = \mu_0 = 25\)

\(H_1: \mu \ne \mu_0 = 25\)

Let's perform a one sample \href{https://www.sthda.com/english/wiki/one-sample-t-test-in-r}{\(t\)-test in R}
(and let's ignore the Shapiro-Wilk test in the link):

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{set.seed}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1234}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{my\_data }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(}
  \AttributeTok{name =} \FunctionTok{paste0}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"M\_"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{), }\DecValTok{1}\SpecialCharTok{:}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{),}
  \AttributeTok{weight =} \FunctionTok{round}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{rnorm}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{), }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{)}
\FunctionTok{head}\NormalTok{(my\_data, }\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##    name weight
## 1   M_1   17.6
## 2   M_2   20.6
## 3   M_3   22.2
## 4   M_4   15.3
## 5   M_5   20.9
## 6   M_6   21.0
## 7   M_7   18.9
## 8   M_8   18.9
## 9   M_9   18.9
## 10 M_10   18.2
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}

\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##    Min. 1st Qu.  Median    Mean 3rd Qu.    Max. 
##   15.30   18.38   18.90   19.25   20.82   22.20
\end{verbatim}

          \AttributeTok{ylab =} \StringTok{"Weight (g)"}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{xlab =} \ConstantTok{FALSE}\NormalTok{,}
          \AttributeTok{ggtheme =} \FunctionTok{theme\_minimal}\NormalTok{())}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]

         \AttributeTok{ggtheme =} \FunctionTok{theme\_minimal}\NormalTok{())}
\end{Highlighting}

##  17.888 20.612

##     19.25
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
