       \AttributeTok{alternative =} \StringTok{"less"}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{conf.level =} \FloatTok{0.94}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  One Sample t-test
## 
## data:  my_data$weight
## t = -9.0783, df = 9, p-value = 3.977e-06
## alternative hypothesis: true mean is less than 25
## 94 percent confidence interval:
##      -Inf 20.33788
## sample estimates:
## mean of x 
##     19.25
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{t.test}\NormalTok{(my\_data}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{weight, }\AttributeTok{mu =} \DecValTok{25}\NormalTok{,}
       \AttributeTok{alternative =} \StringTok{"greater"}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{conf.level =} \FloatTok{0.94}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  One Sample t-test
## 
## data:  my_data$weight
## t = -9.0783, df = 9, p-value = 1
## alternative hypothesis: true mean is greater than 25
## 94 percent confidence interval:
##  18.16212      Inf
## sample estimates:
## mean of x 
##     19.25
\end{verbatim}

Let' verify the test statistic by ``hand'' for the two-sided test:

\[t = \frac{\bar{X} - \mu_0}{s/\sqrt{n}}\]

\(\bar{x} = 19.25\)

\(s = 2.002915\)

\(n = 10\)

\(t = \frac{19.25 - 25}{2.002915/\sqrt{10}} = -9.078319\)

Under the null hypothesis and
\href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Student\%27s_t-test\#Assumptions}{assumptions} met,
the test statistic is distributed according
to a \(t\)-distribution with 9 degrees of freedom.
Hence, the \(p\)-value is:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{pt}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\FloatTok{9.078319}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{df =} \DecValTok{9}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{*} \DecValTok{2} \CommentTok{\# two{-}sided}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 7.953381e-06
\end{verbatim}

This matches the output of the \texttt{t.test} function in R.

\textbf{\href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Student\%27s_t-test\#Assumptions}{Assumptions of the \(t\)-test}}:

You will often see that researchers check if the \emph{data} is normally distributed.
This is not strictly necessary. Merely \(\bar{X}\) must be normally distributed, which is
often guaranteed by the
\href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_limit_theorem}{central limit theorem} for a
large enough sample size \(n\).

\textbf{IF the data is normally distributed, then \(\bar{X}\) is exactly normally distributed}.
This is always true
and the reason for testing for normality of the data.

Another assumption is that \(\frac{s^2(n-1)}{\sigma^2} \sim \chi^2_{n-1}\)

\textbf{Let's try to verify this assumption empirically:}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Load necessary libraries}
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(ggplot2)}

\CommentTok{\# Parameters}
\NormalTok{n }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \DecValTok{10}            \CommentTok{\# Sample size}
\NormalTok{mu\_0 }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \DecValTok{25}         \CommentTok{\# Mean under null hypothesis}
\NormalTok{sigma }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \DecValTok{1}         \CommentTok{\# Standard deviation}
\NormalTok{num\_simulations }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \DecValTok{10000}  \CommentTok{\# Number of simulations}

\CommentTok{\# Simulations}
\FunctionTok{set.seed}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{42}\NormalTok{)  }\CommentTok{\# For reproducibility}
\NormalTok{chi\_squared\_values }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{numeric}\NormalTok{(num\_simulations)}

\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ (i }\ControlFlowTok{in} \DecValTok{1}\SpecialCharTok{:}\NormalTok{num\_simulations) \{}
  \CommentTok{\# Generate random sample}
\NormalTok{  sample }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{rnorm}\NormalTok{(n, }\AttributeTok{mean =}\NormalTok{ mu\_0, }\AttributeTok{sd =}\NormalTok{ sigma)}
  \CommentTok{\# Calculate sample variance}
\NormalTok{  sample\_variance }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{var}\NormalTok{(sample)}
  \CommentTok{\# Calculate chi{-}squared value}
\NormalTok{  chi\_squared\_values[i] }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\NormalTok{ (n }\SpecialCharTok{{-}} \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{*}\NormalTok{ sample\_variance }\SpecialCharTok{/}\NormalTok{ sigma}\SpecialCharTok{\^{}}\DecValTok{2}
\NormalTok{\}}

\FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{chi\_squared =}\NormalTok{ chi\_squared\_values) }\SpecialCharTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}
  \FunctionTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{aes}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{x =}\NormalTok{ chi\_squared)) }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \FunctionTok{geom\_histogram}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{aes}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{y =} \FunctionTok{after\_stat}\NormalTok{(density)), }\AttributeTok{bins =} \DecValTok{50}\NormalTok{,}
                 \AttributeTok{fill =} \StringTok{"lightblue"}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{color =} \StringTok{"white"}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \FunctionTok{stat\_function}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{fun =}\NormalTok{ dchisq, }\AttributeTok{args =} \FunctionTok{list}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{df =}\NormalTok{ n }\SpecialCharTok{{-}} \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{),}
                \AttributeTok{color =} \StringTok{"red"}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{linewidth =} \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \FunctionTok{labs}\NormalTok{(}
    \AttributeTok{title =} \FunctionTok{expression}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Empirical Verification of "} \SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{ chi}\SpecialCharTok{\^{}}\DecValTok{2} \SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}} \StringTok{" Distribution"}\NormalTok{),}
    \AttributeTok{x =} \StringTok{"Value"}\NormalTok{,}
    \AttributeTok{y =} \StringTok{"Density"}
\NormalTok{  ) }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \FunctionTok{theme\_minimal}\NormalTok{() }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \FunctionTok{theme}\NormalTok{(}
    \AttributeTok{plot.title =} \FunctionTok{element\_text}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{hjust =} \FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{  )}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{_main_files/_main_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-63-1.pdf}}

This assumption seems to be met.

The last assumption listed is that \(Z = \bar{X} - \mu\) and \(s\) are independent.
\(Z\) measures how far \(\bar{X}\) is from \(\mu\). Independence would mean here
that irrespective of how far away a sample is from the true mean
(respectively the mean under \(H_0\)), the sample variance
has the same distribution.

One could verify this theoretically or by applying an
\href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hoeffding\%27s_independence_test}{independence test}.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Two sample \(t\)-test}{Two sample t-test}}\label{two-sample-t-test}

\(H_0: \mu_1 = \mu_2\)

\(H_1: \mu_1 \ne \mu_2\)

Let's jump right in and use our example from the previous chapter where we performed the Bayesian \(t\)-test:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{(y1 }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\NormalTok{.}\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{1.2}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{1.2}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{1.2}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{1.9}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{2.4}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{*} \DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] -50   0 120 120 120 190 240 300
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{(y2 }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\FloatTok{1.2}\NormalTok{, }\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\FloatTok{1.2}\NormalTok{, }\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\NormalTok{.}\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, .}\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{1.1}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{1.9}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{*} \DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] -120 -120  -50    0    0   50  110  190
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{length}\NormalTok{(y1)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 8
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{length}\NormalTok{(y2)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 8
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{data }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(y1, y2)}
\NormalTok{psych}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{describe}\NormalTok{(data)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##    vars n  mean     sd median trimmed    mad  min max range  skew kurtosis
## y1    1 8 130.0 116.00    120   130.0 140.85  -50 300   350 -0.13    -1.39
## y2    2 8   7.5 107.94      0     7.5 118.61 -120 190   310  0.29    -1.38
##       se
## y1 41.01
## y2 38.16
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Boxplot:}
\FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{y =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(y1, y2), }\AttributeTok{group =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{8}\NormalTok{), }\FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{8}\NormalTok{))) }\SpecialCharTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}
  \FunctionTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{aes}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{x =} \FunctionTok{factor}\NormalTok{(group), }\AttributeTok{y =}\NormalTok{ y)) }\SpecialCharTok{+}  \CommentTok{\# Use factor for discrete x{-}axis}
  \FunctionTok{geom\_boxplot}\NormalTok{() }\SpecialCharTok{+}                        \CommentTok{\# Add boxplot layer}
  \FunctionTok{geom\_jitter}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{width =} \FloatTok{0.1}\NormalTok{)                }\CommentTok{\# Add jitter for individual}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{_main_files/_main_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-64-1.pdf}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# {-}\textgreater{} Visually, there seems to be a difference between the two groups.}

\FunctionTok{t.test}\NormalTok{(y1, y2, }\AttributeTok{conf.level =} \FloatTok{0.93}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Welch Two Sample t-test
## 
## data:  y1 and y2
## t = 2.1867, df = 13.928, p-value = 0.04634
## alternative hypothesis: true difference in means is not equal to 0
## 93 percent confidence interval:
##   12.55847 232.44153
## sample estimates:
## mean of x mean of y 
##     130.0       7.5
\end{verbatim}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  The test statistic is a \(t\)-distribution with \(13.928\) degrees of freedom (under \(H_0\)).
\item
  The value of the test statistic is \(2.1867\).
\item
  \(H_0: \mu_1 = \mu_2\). The group means are equal.
\item
  \(H_1: \mu_1 \ne \mu_2\). The group means are different.
\item
  \(p\)-value \(= 0.04634\). In the classical framework,
  this would be considered ``significant'' at the \(\alpha = 0.05\) level
  and one would reject the null hypothesis and accept the alternative hypothesis.
  \textbf{In the Baysian framework, we abstained from making a decision}.
\item
  93\% confidence interval for the difference in means is rather wide:
  \(12.55847\) to \(232.44153\).

  Let's try to visualize this.

  \textbf{Under the assumption} that there is \textbf{no difference in means},
  the \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Student\%27s_t-test\#Equal_or_unequal_sample_sizes,_unequal_variances_(sX1_\%3E_2sX2_or_sX2_\%3E_2sX1)}{test statistic}

  \[ t = \frac{\bar{X}_1 - \bar{X}_2}{\sqrt{\frac{s_1^2}{n_1} + \frac{s_2^2}{n_2}}} \]

  would be distributed according to a \(t\)-distribution with 13.928 degrees of freedom.
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Load ggplot2}
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(ggplot2)}

\CommentTok{\# Define degrees of freedom}
\NormalTok{df }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FloatTok{13.928}

\CommentTok{\# Define the range for x and the critical t{-}values}
\NormalTok{x }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{seq}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{length.out =} \DecValTok{500}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{critical\_t }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FloatTok{2.1867}

\CommentTok{\# Create a data frame with x and corresponding density values}
\NormalTok{t\_dist }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(}
  \AttributeTok{x =}\NormalTok{ x,}
  \AttributeTok{density =} \FunctionTok{dt}\NormalTok{(x, df)}
\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Plot the t{-}distribution with shaded tail areas}
\FunctionTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(t\_dist, }\FunctionTok{aes}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{x =}\NormalTok{ x, }\AttributeTok{y =}\NormalTok{ density)) }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \CommentTok{\# Add the main t{-}distribution curve}
  \FunctionTok{geom\_line}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{linewidth =} \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{color =} \StringTok{"blue"}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \CommentTok{\# Add shaded areas below the curve outside the critical t{-}values}
  \FunctionTok{geom\_ribbon}\NormalTok{(}
    \AttributeTok{data =} \FunctionTok{subset}\NormalTok{(t\_dist, x }\SpecialCharTok{\textless{}} \SpecialCharTok{{-}}\NormalTok{critical\_t),}
    \FunctionTok{aes}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{ymin =} \DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{ymax =}\NormalTok{ density),}
    \AttributeTok{fill =} \StringTok{"red"}\NormalTok{,}
    \AttributeTok{alpha =} \FloatTok{0.3}
\NormalTok{  ) }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \FunctionTok{geom\_ribbon}\NormalTok{(}
    \AttributeTok{data =} \FunctionTok{subset}\NormalTok{(t\_dist, x }\SpecialCharTok{\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ critical\_t),}
    \FunctionTok{aes}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{ymin =} \DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{ymax =}\NormalTok{ density),}
    \AttributeTok{fill =} \StringTok{"red"}\NormalTok{,}
    \AttributeTok{alpha =} \FloatTok{0.3}
\NormalTok{  ) }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \CommentTok{\# Add vertical lines for the critical t{-}values}
  \FunctionTok{geom\_vline}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{xintercept =} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\NormalTok{critical\_t, critical\_t),}
             \AttributeTok{linetype =} \StringTok{"dashed"}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{color =} \StringTok{"black"}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \CommentTok{\# Annotate the critical t{-}values}
  \FunctionTok{annotate}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"text"}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{x =} \SpecialCharTok{{-}}\NormalTok{critical\_t, }\AttributeTok{y =} \FloatTok{0.05}\NormalTok{,}
           \AttributeTok{label =} \FunctionTok{paste0}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"{-}t = "}\NormalTok{, critical\_t), }\AttributeTok{angle =} \DecValTok{90}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{vjust =} \SpecialCharTok{{-}}\FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \FunctionTok{annotate}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"text"}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{x =}\NormalTok{ critical\_t, }\AttributeTok{y =} \FloatTok{0.05}\NormalTok{,}
           \AttributeTok{label =} \FunctionTok{paste0}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"t = "}\NormalTok{, critical\_t), }\AttributeTok{angle =} \DecValTok{90}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{vjust =} \SpecialCharTok{{-}}\FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \CommentTok{\# Add labels and style}
  \FunctionTok{labs}\NormalTok{(}
    \AttributeTok{title =} \StringTok{"t{-}Distribution with Shaded Critical Areas (Two{-}Sided Test)"}\NormalTok{,}
    \AttributeTok{subtitle =} \FunctionTok{paste}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Degrees of Freedom:"}\NormalTok{, df),}
    \AttributeTok{x =} \StringTok{"t"}\NormalTok{,}
    \AttributeTok{y =} \StringTok{"Density"}
\NormalTok{  ) }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \FunctionTok{theme\_minimal}\NormalTok{() }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \FunctionTok{theme}\NormalTok{(}
    \AttributeTok{plot.title =} \FunctionTok{element\_text}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{hjust =} \FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{),  }\CommentTok{\# Center the title}
    \AttributeTok{plot.subtitle =} \FunctionTok{element\_text}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{hjust =} \FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{)  }\CommentTok{\# Center the subtitle}
\NormalTok{  )}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{_main_files/_main_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-65-1.pdf}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# p{-}value manually:}
\FunctionTok{pt}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\FloatTok{2.1867}\NormalTok{, df) }\SpecialCharTok{*} \DecValTok{2}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 0.04633225
\end{verbatim}

The plot shows the \(t\)-distribution. Marked in red are the areas where the test statistic
takes the value we observed or ``more extreme'' values. With ``more extreme'' we mean values that are
further away from 0 in both directions since we conducted a two-sided test. The area under the curve
is the \(p\)-value. As you can see, the \(p\)-value is the sum of the two red areas and matches the output
of the \(t\)-test function in R.

Interesting: \href{https://github.com/jdegenfellner/ZHAW_Teaching/blob/main/Influence_of_outlieres_on_t-Test.R}{This R-file} demonstrates what one single outlier can do to a \(t\)-test.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Paired \(t\)-test}{Paired t-test}}\label{paired-t-test}

In the last case, we assume, that the two samples are \emph{not} independent anymore. We could, for instance,
think of two weight measurements (kg) of the same person before and after a diet. Obviously,
these two measurements are not independent. Higher pre-diet weights could be associated
with higher post-diet weights.

Analog to before, we now ask if the differences are ``significantly'' different from \(\mu_0\).

In the bodyweight example, let's define a 5 kg weight loss as clinically relevant
(in reality this is more like 5\%), but we keep it simple.

\(H_0: \mu_D \le \mu_0 = 5~kg\). Participants lost 5 kg or less on average,
i.e.~the true difference between pre- and post weight is on average smaller than 5kg.

\(H_1: \mu_D > \mu_0 = 5~kg\). Participants lost more than 5 kg on average.

Aussumptions for the paired \(t\)-test:
- Interval or ratio scale
- The differences are independent of each other
-

Let's create some data and perform the test:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(pacman)}
\FunctionTok{p\_load}\NormalTok{(MASS, tidyverse)}

\CommentTok{\# Create correlated pre post weight{-}data}
\FunctionTok{set.seed}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{62}\NormalTok{)  }\CommentTok{\# For reproducibility}
\NormalTok{n }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \DecValTok{15}  \CommentTok{\# Number of pairs}
\NormalTok{mu }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{80}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{73}\NormalTok{)  }\CommentTok{\# Mean vector for Pre and Post weights}
\NormalTok{rho }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FloatTok{0.7}  \CommentTok{\# Correlation}
\NormalTok{sigma }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \DecValTok{10}  \CommentTok{\# Standard deviation for both variables}

\NormalTok{Sigma }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{matrix}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(sigma}\SpecialCharTok{\^{}}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, rho }\SpecialCharTok{*}\NormalTok{ sigma}\SpecialCharTok{\^{}}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, rho }\SpecialCharTok{*}\NormalTok{ sigma}\SpecialCharTok{\^{}}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, sigma}\SpecialCharTok{\^{}}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{), }\AttributeTok{nrow =} \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{weights }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{mvrnorm}\NormalTok{(n, }\AttributeTok{mu =}\NormalTok{ mu, }\AttributeTok{Sigma =}\NormalTok{ Sigma)}

\NormalTok{df }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(}
  \AttributeTok{Pre\_Weight =} \FunctionTok{round}\NormalTok{(weights[, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{], }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{),}
  \AttributeTok{Post\_Weight =} \FunctionTok{round}\NormalTok{(weights[, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{], }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{)}

\end{Highlighting}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 0.2499412
\end{verbatim}

Conclusion: The observed mean difference in weights is explainable by chance alone (\(p\)-value rather high).
We continue to believe in \(H_0\).

\section{Correlation test}\label{correlation-test}

In the chapter about descriptive statistics, we calculated the (Pearson) correlation coefficient
to measure the strength of the \textbf{linear} relationship between two variables.

Often, the null hypothesis for the correlation coefficient is that there is no correlation between
the two variables (\(\rho=0\)). One could argue that this is a rather baseless assumption. In reality,
the true correlation coefficient is probably not exactly 0 and one could argue more precisely a range
of plausible values for \(\rho\) for the specific case at hand. Often, one can see an ocean of \(p\)-values
in the literature, where the correlation coefficient is tested against 0. This is superfluous.
For example, if the sample size is \(n=234\) and the sample correlation coefficient is \(r = 0.76\), it
is very unlikely that the true correlation coefficient is 0. One does not need a hypothesis test to
know this (see \hyperref[exercise6_nhst]{exercise 6}).

\subsection{Classical correlation test}\label{classical-correlation-test}

We can do a correlation test in R with the \texttt{cor.test} function.
\href{https://www.sthda.com/english/wiki/correlation-test-between-two-variables-in-r}{This article} is also helpful.
Maybe, let's take the part about the Shapiro-Wilk test not too seriously.

The test statistic(s) for the test(s) can be found \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pearson_correlation_coefficient\#Inference}{here}.
If we really want to take the result of such a test seriously, we need to check the assumptions of the test.
\href{https://core.ac.uk/download/pdf/234680353.pdf}{This} might be an interesting read.

The correlation plot from the \href{https://www.sthda.com/english/wiki/correlation-test-between-two-variables-in-r}{article} looks like this:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(ggpubr)}
\NormalTok{mtcars }\SpecialCharTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}
  \FunctionTok{ggscatter}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{x =} \StringTok{"mpg"}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{y =} \StringTok{"wt"}\NormalTok{,}
            \AttributeTok{add =} \StringTok{"reg.line"}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{conf.int =} \ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{,}
            \AttributeTok{cor.coef =} \ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{cor.method =} \StringTok{"pearson"}\NormalTok{,}
            \AttributeTok{xlab =} \StringTok{"Miles/(US) gallon"}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{ylab =} \StringTok{"Weight (1000 lbs)"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{_main_files/_main_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-68-1.pdf}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{cor.test}\NormalTok{(mtcars}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{mpg, mtcars}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{wt, }\AttributeTok{conf.level =} \FloatTok{0.96}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Pearson's product-moment correlation
## 
## data:  mtcars$mpg and mtcars$wt
## t = -9.559, df = 30, p-value = 1.294e-10
## alternative hypothesis: true correlation is not equal to 0
## 96 percent confidence interval:
##  -0.9360192 -0.7362129
## sample estimates:
##        cor 
## -0.8676594
\end{verbatim}

\(r=-0.87\) is the sample correlation coefficient.
Interestingly, \texttt{cor.test} in R can only test \(H_0: \rho = 0\).

In the scatter plot, we can see a linear relationship between the two variables.
The correlation might therefore be a useful measure to describe the relationship between the two variables.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  t is the \(t\)-test statistic value (t = -9.559),
\item
  df is the degrees of freedom (df= 30),
\item
  \(p\)-value of the \(t\)-test (\(p\)-value = \(1.29410^{-10}\)).
\item
  conf.int is the confidence interval of the correlation coefficient at 96\% (conf.int = \([-0.9360192, -0.7362129]\))
  This is what is most interesting to us and should be interpreted in the context of the data.
\item
  sample estimates is the correlation coefficient (\(r = -0.87\)).
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Let's verify the test statistic for the two-sided test by ``hand'':}

For pairs \textbf{from an uncorrelated bivariate normal distribution}, the sampling distribution
of the studentized Pearson's correlation coefficient follows a \(t\)-distribution with degrees of freedom \(n − 2\).
Specifically, if the underlying variables have a bivariate normal distribution, the variable

\[t = \frac{r \sqrt{n-2}}{\sqrt{1-r^2}}\]

has a student's \(t\)-distribution in the null case (zero correlation).

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{(r }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{cor}\NormalTok{(mtcars}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{mpg, mtcars}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{wt))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] -0.8676594
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{(n }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{nrow}\NormalTok{(mtcars))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 32
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{(t }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\NormalTok{ r }\SpecialCharTok{*} \FunctionTok{sqrt}\NormalTok{(n }\SpecialCharTok{{-}} \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{/} \FunctionTok{sqrt}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1} \SpecialCharTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ r}\SpecialCharTok{\^{}}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] -9.559044
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{(p }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \DecValTok{2} \SpecialCharTok{*} \FunctionTok{pt}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\FunctionTok{abs}\NormalTok{(t), }\AttributeTok{df =}\NormalTok{ n }\SpecialCharTok{{-}} \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 1.293959e-10
\end{verbatim}

This matches the output of the \texttt{cor.test} function in R.

Study this \href{https://github.com/jdegenfellner/ZHAW_Teaching/blob/main/Variability_of_Correlation_under_H_0.R}{file}
to understand the variability of the sample correlation coefficient under the null hypothesis (\(\rho=0\)).
Please be aware of this variability when interpreting the results of your master thesis.

\subsection{Bootstrap confidence interval for the correlation coefficient}\label{bootstrap-confidence-interval-for-the-correlation-coefficient}

We could also use the so-called \textbf{simple non-parametric \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bootstrapping_(statistics)}{bootstrap}} to estimate the confidence interval for the correlation coefficient.
These are the steps:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Create a new sample by \href{https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4a/Illustration_bootstrap.svg}{drawing with replacement} from the original sample.
\item
  Repeat this process many times (e.g., 1000 times).
\item
  Calculate the correlation coefficient for each new sample.
\item
  Calculate adequate sample quantiles of the correlation coefficients to get a confidence interval.
\end{itemize}

There is a simple elegance to this method. It is very general and can be applied to many different problems.

Let's apply it to finding a confidence interval for the correlation coefficient between \texttt{mpg} and \texttt{wt} in the \texttt{mtcars} dataset.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Bootstrap confidence interval for the correlation coefficient}
\FunctionTok{set.seed}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{123}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{cors }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{replicate}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1000}\NormalTok{, \{}
\NormalTok{  idx }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{sample}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\SpecialCharTok{:}\NormalTok{n, n, }\AttributeTok{replace =} \ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{)}
  \FunctionTok{cor}\NormalTok{(mtcars}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{mpg[idx], mtcars}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{wt[idx])}
\NormalTok{\})}
\FunctionTok{hist}\NormalTok{(cors)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{_main_files/_main_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-70-1.pdf}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{quantile}\NormalTok{(cors, }\FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\FloatTok{0.02}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{0.98}\NormalTok{)) }\CommentTok{\# 96\% confidence interval}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##         2%        50%        98% 
## -0.9260149 -0.8745279 -0.7821605
\end{verbatim}

We used the variability \textbf{contained in the data} to estimate the variability of
the correlation coefficient. As you can see in the histrogram, correlations close to 0 are
very unlikely. The 96\% bootstrap confidence interval is \([-0.926, -0.782]\).

You can also study the nice animation of the bootstrap \href{https://seeing-theory.brown.edu/frequentist-inference/index.html}{here}.

\subsection{Comparison with Bayesian approach}\label{comparison-with-bayesian-approach}

For the Bayesian approach, we can use the \texttt{correlationBF} function from
the \texttt{BayesFactor} package. In analogy to the classical test, we can ``test'' the null hypothesis
by assuming a prior distribution for the correlation coefficient symmetric around the
value 0 (slee prior plot below).

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(BayesFactor)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\end{verbatim}

\end{verbatim}

\end{verbatim}

##

\end{verbatim}

\end{verbatim}

\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{library}\NormalTok{(tidyverse)}

\FunctionTok{set.seed}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{444}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Define a function for the shifted, scaled Beta prior}
\NormalTok{shifted\_beta\_prior }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \ControlFlowTok{function}\NormalTok{(rho, rscale) \{}
  \CommentTok{\# Transform Beta to [{-}1, 1]}
  \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ (rho }\SpecialCharTok{\textgreater{}=} \SpecialCharTok{{-}}\DecValTok{1} \SpecialCharTok{\&\&}\NormalTok{ rho }\SpecialCharTok{\textless{}=} \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{) \{}
    \CommentTok{\# (rho + 1) / 2 transforms [{-}1, 1] to [0, 1]}
\NormalTok{    beta\_density }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{dbeta}\NormalTok{((rho }\SpecialCharTok{+} \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{/} \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1} \SpecialCharTok{/}\NormalTok{ rscale, }\DecValTok{1} \SpecialCharTok{/}\NormalTok{ rscale) }\SpecialCharTok{/} \DecValTok{2}
    \FunctionTok{return}\NormalTok{(beta\_density)}
\NormalTok{  \} }\ControlFlowTok{else}\NormalTok{ \{}
    \FunctionTok{return}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{  \}}
\NormalTok{\}}

\CommentTok{\# Define rho values and rscale}
\NormalTok{rho\_values }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{seq}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{by =} \FloatTok{0.01}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{rscale }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \DecValTok{1} \SpecialCharTok{/} \DecValTok{3}  \CommentTok{\# Medium scale}

\CommentTok{\# Compute prior values}
\NormalTok{prior\_values }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{sapply}\NormalTok{(rho\_values, shifted\_beta\_prior, }\AttributeTok{rscale =}\NormalTok{ rscale)}

\CommentTok{\# Plot the prior}
\FunctionTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{rho =}\NormalTok{ rho\_values, }\AttributeTok{density =}\NormalTok{ prior\_values) }\SpecialCharTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}
\FunctionTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{aes}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{x =}\NormalTok{ rho, }\AttributeTok{y =}\NormalTok{ density)) }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \FunctionTok{geom\_line}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{color =} \StringTok{"blue"}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{linewidth =} \FloatTok{1.2}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \FunctionTok{labs}\NormalTok{(}
    \AttributeTok{title =} \StringTok{"Shifted Scaled Beta Prior Distribution for Correlation"}\NormalTok{,}

    \AttributeTok{y =} \StringTok{"Density"}
\NormalTok{  ) }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \FunctionTok{theme\_minimal}\NormalTok{() }\SpecialCharTok{+}
  \FunctionTok{theme}\NormalTok{(}
    \AttributeTok{plot.title =} \FunctionTok{element\_text}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{hjust =} \FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{size =} \DecValTok{16}\NormalTok{),}
    \AttributeTok{text =} \FunctionTok{element\_text}\NormalTok{(}\AttributeTok{size =} \DecValTok{14}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{  )}
\end{Highlighting}

## 96% Credible Interval for rho: -0.9083272 to -0.6384908
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] -0.8159496
\end{verbatim}

The following intervals were obtained in three different ways:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Classical frequentist:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    96\% confidence interval: \([-0.9360192, -0.7362129]\)
  \item
    Estimate: \(-0.8676594\)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Bootstrap:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    96\% bootstrap confidence interval: \([-0.926, -0.782]\)
  \item
    Median of the bootstrap samples: \(-0.8745279\)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Bayesian:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    96\% credible interval: \([-0.9083272, -0.6384908]\)
  \item
    Median of the posterior samples: \(-0.8159496\)
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\href{https://seeing-theory.brown.edu/frequentist-inference/index.html}{This}
website should be fun to explore.

\section{Type 1 and Type 2 errors}\label{error_types}

Decision about null hypothesis (H0)

Null hypothesis (H0) is true

Null hypothesis (H0) is false

Not reject

Correct inference (true negative)
(probability = 1 - α)

Type II error (false negative)
(probability = β)

Reject

Type I error (false positive)
(probability = α)

Correct inference (true positive)
(probability = 1 - β)

In the dichotomous world of hypothesis testing, we can make \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Type_I_and_type_II_errors}{two types of errors}:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  A type 1 error (false positive) occurs when we reject the null hypothesis when it is actually true.
\item
  A type 2 error (false negative) occurs when we do not reject the null hypothesis when it is actually false.
\end{itemize}

The expression \(1 - \beta\) is called the \textbf{power} of the test.
It is the probability of correctly rejecting the null hypothesis when it is false.

\href{https://github.com/jdegenfellner/ZHAW_Teaching/blob/main/H_0_and_the_truth.R}{This} R-file
could be interesting to study with respect to \(H_0\) and the probability of finding the truth.

\textbf{How to choose \(\alpha\) and \(\beta\)?}

So far, I have not seen this question answered in a practical way in papers.
One typically reads
that ``the level of statistical significance is set at \(\alpha = 0.05\)'', which is arbitrary.

\href{https://journals.plos.org/plosone/article?id=10.1371/journal.pone.0032734}{Mudge et al.~(2012)}
answer the question like this:
``Thus, the logical decision-making significance threshold, \(\alpha\),
should be the value that minimizes the probability, or occasionally,
the cost of making any relevant error.''

We have encountered the argument, that Bayesian statistics is subjective.
Unfortunately, a similar problem arises in NHST. To quote Mudge et al.~again:

``\ldots{} consistently using \(\alpha = 0.05\) is not an objective approach. Subjectivity is
merely shifted away from the choice of \(\alpha\) to the choice of sample size, such
that if a researcher wants to find statistical significance using \(\alpha = 0.05\),
they should conduct a test with a large sample size \ldots{}''

For the case of equal a priori probability of the \(H_0\) and \(H_1\) and equal consequence
for both types or errors, the suggest to \href{https://journals.plos.org/plosone/article/figure?id=10.1371/journal.pone.0032734.g002}{choose alpha by minimizing}

\[\omega = \frac{\alpha + \beta}{2}.\]

\section{The frequentist confidence interval}\label{the-frequentist-confidence-interval}

\textbf{In frequentist statistics, a \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Confidence_interval}{confidence interval (CI)} is an interval which is expected to
typically (with repeated sampling) contain the true but unknown parameter with a certain frequency (probability).}

It is very important to understand that for every sample, the confidence interval is different and in the long run,
these intervals will contain the true parameter in a certain percentage of cases.

This is a \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Confidence_interval\#/media/File:Normal_distribution_50\%25_CI_illustration.svg}{nice visualization}
of the concept.
See also \hyperref[exercise7_nhst]{exercise 7}.

\textbf{1:1 relationship between \(p\)-values and confidence intervals:}

There is a 1:1 relationship between ``statistical significance'' and confidence intervals.

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Example 1: If we test (\(\alpha = 0.06\)) the null hypothesis that the mean of a population
  is 0 (\(H_1: \mu \ne 0\)),
  and we get a \(p\)-value of 0.0298, then the 94\% confidence interval \textbf{for the mean}
  will not contain 0.
\item
  Example 2: If we test (\(\alpha = 0.07\)) the null hypothesis that means of two independent
  populations are equal (\(H_1: \mu_1 \ne \mu_2\)), and we get a \(p\)-value of 0.0433,
  then the 93\% confidence interval for the \textbf{difference in means} will not contain 0.
\item
  Example 3: If we test (\(\alpha = 0.05\)) the null hypothesis that the risk ratio for
  lung cancer equas 1 in smokers vs.~non-smokers (\(H_1: RR \ne 1\)), and we get a
  \(p\)-value of 0.00032, then the 97\% confidence interval for the \textbf{risk ratio (RR)}
  will not contain 1.
\end{itemize}

\section{Simulations based approaches}\label{simulations-based-approaches}

Through the power of modern computers, we can simulate all kinds of hypothesis tests.
We just assume that the null hypothesis is true and draw samples from the very distribution.
In the old times, computational resources were scarce and one had to rely on
\href{https://digital.library.adelaide.edu.au/server/api/core/bitstreams/21f22d02-4a39-4650-8f09-71434ad02897/content}{tables}
that were precalculated.

\href{https://github.com/jdegenfellner/ZHAW_Teaching/blob/main/Variability_of_Correlation_under_H_0.R}{This}
shows you how the correlation coefficient behaves under the null hypothesis (that there is no correlation; \(\rho = 0\)).
This is a very useful tool to understand the behavior of a test statistic under the null hypothesis.
Of course, the probability that a correlation coefficient is exactly 0 is 0 since it's a continuous variable,
but in practice we are interested in ``indistinguishable from 0'' which is a small value.
In the Bayesian framework from Kruschke, we defined a region of practical equivalence (ROPE) for this purpose.

\textbf{We ask ourselves}: What does my test statistic do \emph{if} the null hypothesis is true?
Then try to simulate it. This helps to understand what constitutes a qualitatively different result
(from the null hypothesis) considering variability in the data.

\section{Exercises}\label{exercises-3}

\subsection{Exercise 1 - frequentist confidence interval}\label{exercise1_nhst}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Create 1000 random samples from a binomial distribution with \(n = 100\) and \(p = 0.38\).
\item
  Calculate the 96\% confidence interval for each sample using R.
\item
  How often was the true parameter (\(p = 0.38\)) contained in the constructed interval?
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Exercise 2 - everything becomes ``significant''}\label{exercise2_nhst}

Setting: two sample \(t\)-test. Assume there is a small difference between the means of two groups.
- Show via simulation that with increasing sample size, the \(p\)-value becomes smaller and
smaller and will be ``significant'' at some point irresespective of how small the true mean
difference is and how small the \(\alpha-\) level is.

\subsection{Exercise 3 - binomial test}\label{exercise3_nhst}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Create a sample from a binomial distribution with \(n = 54\) and \(p = 0.68\).
\item
  Perform a two-sided binomial test with \(H_0: p = 0.5\).
\item
  Calculate the 90\% confidence interval for the sample proportion.
\item
  Calculate the p-value for the two-sided test by ``hand'' (using dbinom/pbinom in R).
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Exercise 4 - proportions test}\label{exercise4_nhst}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Create a sample from a binomial distribution with \(n = 100\) and \(p = 0.5\).
\item
  Perform a proportions test with \(H_0: p = 0.5\) and interpret the results.
\item
  Perform the proportions test with the whole range of possible proportions
  \(H_0: p = 0.01 \cdots p = 0.99\) in steps of \(0.01\).
  And plot the p-values on the y-axis and
  the assumed proportion on the x-axis.
  This is called a \href{https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/00031305.2018.1556735\#:~:text=The\%20p\%2Dvalue\%20function\%20provides,for\%20the\%20parameter\%20of\%20interest.}{\(p\)-value function}.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Exercise 5 - proportions test 2}\label{exercise5_nhst}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Use the data from the smokers proportions test \hyperref[proportions_test_more_samples]{example above}.
\item
  Draw a \(\chi^2\) distribution with 3 degrees of freedom and calculate the
  probability of observing a value of 12.6 or larger.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Exercise 6 - correlation coefficient}\label{exercise6_nhst}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Create a sample of \(234\) pairs of uncorrelated observations \((x_i,y_i)\).
  \(X_i\) and \(Y_i\) are drawn from a normal distribution with mean 0 and standard deviation 1.
\item
  Calculate the sample correlation coefficient \(r\).
\item
  Repeat this 1000 times.
\item
  How often was the sample correlation coefficient larger than 0.76?
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Exercise 7 - coverage frequency of CI}\label{exercise7_nhst}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Create a sample (vary the sample size, start small) from a normal distribution with mean 0 and standard deviation 1.
\item
  Calculate the 93\% confidence interval for the mean.
\item
  Repeat this 1000 times.
\item
  How often was the true mean (0) contained in the constructed interval?
\end{itemize}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Exercise 8 - \(\chi^2\)-distribution}{Exercise 8 - \textbackslash chi\^{}2-distribution}}\label{exercise8_nhst}

The \(\chi^2\)-distribution is defined as the sum of squared standard normals.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Very this by simulation in R.
\item
  Draw 1000 samples of size 3 from a standard normal distribution.
\item
  Calculate the sum of squared values for each sample.
\item
  Do this repeatedly and plot the histogram of the resulting values with the \(\chi^2\)-density in the diagram.
\item
  Use Q-Q plots to compare the two distributions. The distribution should be a \(\chi^2\)-distribution with 3 degrees of freedom.
\end{itemize}

\section{Sample exam questions for this chapter (in German since exam is in German)}\label{sample-exam-questions-for-this-chapter-in-german-since-exam-is-in-german-3}

For this section, no solutions are provided.

\subsection{Frage 1}\label{frage-1-1}

Welche der folgenden Aussage(n) über Nullhypothesen-Signifikanztests (NHST) ist/sind korrekt (0-4 korrekte Antworten)?

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Der \(p\)-Wert gibt die Wahrscheinlichkeit an, eine Teststatistik zu erhalten,
  die genauso extrem (oder extremer) ist wie die beobachtete, vorausgesetzt, die Nullhypothese ist wahr.
\item
  NHST ist darauf ausgelegt, zu beweisen, dass die Alternativhypothese wahr ist.
\item
  Dichotomes Denken im NHST vereinfacht die Komplexität von realen Daten zu stark.
\item
  Das Signifikanzniveau (\(\alpha\)) gibt die Wahrscheinlichkeit dafür an, dass man die
  Nullhypothese (\(H_0\)) beibehält, obwohl die Alternativhypothese (\(H_1\)) wahr ist.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Frage 2}\label{frage-2-1}

Wir ziehen eine Stichprobe mit 3 Werten und erhalten folgendes Ergebnis: \(x = (4,4,4)\).
In der Vergangenheit gingen wir davon aus, dass die Zufallsvariable \(X\) folgendermaßen verteilt ist:
\[
\begin{array}{|c|c|}
\hline
x & P(X = x) \\
\hline
1 & 0.2 \\
2 & 0.3 \\
3 & 0.4 \\
4 & 0.1 \\
\hline
\end{array}
\]

Welche der folgenden Aussage(n) ist/sind korrekt (0-4 korrekte Antworten)?

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Die Wahrscheinlichkeit, dieses Ereignis unter der Annahme der Gültigkeit obiger Verteilung zu beobachten
  ist \(10^{-4}\).
\item
  Unter der Annahme der Gültigkeit obiger Verteilung wäre die Wahrscheinlichkeit mindestens einmal
  \(1,2\) oder \(3\) zu sehen 99,9\%.
\item
  Wählt man als Teststatistik die Anzahl der \(4\)er in der Stichprobe, so ist die Teststatistik
  unter der Annahme der Gültigkeit obiger Verteilung binomialverteilt.
\item
  Über die Stichprobe \((1,1,1)\) wäre man weniger überrascht als über die Stichprobe \((4,4,4)\).
\end{itemize}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Frage 3 - \(t\)-Test}{Frage 3 - t-Test}}\label{frage-3---t-test}

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  Two Sample t-test
## 
## data:  group_a and group_b
## t = -0.10816, df = 58, p-value = 0.5429
## alternative hypothesis: true difference in means is greater than 0
## 95 percent confidence interval:
##  -0.4186255        Inf
## sample estimates:
## mean of x mean of y 
##  4.952896  4.978338
\end{verbatim}

Welche der folgenden Aussage(n) ist/sind korrekt (0-4 korrekte Antworten)?

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Es handelt sich um einen zweiseitigen \(t\)-Test.
\item
  Aufgrund des \(p\)-Wertes würde man in diesem Fall die Nullhypothese nicht verwerfen.
\item
  Die Alternativhypothese lautet: \(H_1: \mu_{group\_a} - \mu_{group\_b} > 0\).
\item
  Die Nullhypothese lautet: \(H_0: \mu_{group\_a} = \mu_{group\_b}\).
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Frage 4}\label{frage-4-1}

Wir führen einen Hypothesentest für Proportionen in R durch (\(n=100\)).
Hinweis: Nicht den \(p\)-Wert mit dem Parameter p (der gesuchten Proportion) verwechseln.

\begin{verbatim}
## 
##  1-sample proportions test without continuity correction
## 
## data:  x out of n, null probability p_null
## X-squared = 6.4533, df = 1, p-value = 0.01107
## alternative hypothesis: true p is not equal to 0.75
## 97 percent confidence interval:
##  0.5317140 0.7356931
## sample estimates:
##    p 
## 0.64
\end{verbatim}

Welche der folgenden Aussage(n) ist/sind korrekt (0-4 korrekte Antworten)?

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Es waren 64 ``Erfolge'' in 100 Versuchen.
\item
  Die Wahrscheinlichkeit, dass der ware aber unbekannte Parameter p im
  Intervall \([0.5317140, 0.7356931]\) liegt, beträgt 97\%.
\item
  Die Nullhypothese lautet: \(H_0: \text{p} \ge 0.75\).
\item
  Würde man statt 97\% nur 90\% wählen, wäre das Intervall breiter.
\end{itemize}
